Port of Los Angeles May 2026 Cargo News Briefing on Middle East, Tariffs, Supply Chain

Port of Los Angeles(21.3K)·62:56·246 views·2026-05-12
Katherine TaiFormer U.S. Trade Representative
TL;DRThe Port of Los Angeles handled 891,000 TEUs in April 2026—its second-best April on record—amid a landscape defined by extreme geopolitical volatility, shifting tariff authorities, and a 50% surge in diesel prices driven by the ongoing conflict in the Middle East.

TL;DR

The Port of Los Angeles handled 891,000 TEUs in April 2026—its second-best April on record—amid a landscape defined by extreme geopolitical volatility, shifting tariff authorities, and a 50% surge in diesel prices driven by the ongoing conflict in the Middle East.

April 2026 Cargo Performance & Economic Outlook

  • Record-Setting Volume: The port processed 891,000 container units in April, a 5.5% increase over the previous year and the highest monthly total since August 2025.
      • Imports: Robust at 460,000 TEUs (up 1.5% YoY), driven by resilient consumer spending and factories in Asia operating at full capacity for back-to-school and early holiday inventory.
      • Year-to-Date: 3.3 million TEUs have been moved in the first four months of 2026, tracking 2% above the five-year average.
  • Domestic Economic Pressures: While GDP grew at 2% in Q1, inflationary pressures are mounting at the consumer level.
      • Fuel Costs: Diesel prices are up 50%, and Southern California gasoline has reached $6.15 per gallon (a 26% YoY increase).
      • Consumer Behavior: Spending remains near record levels, but shoppers are increasingly "bargain hunting" to offset rising costs.

Trade Policy and Regulatory Volatility

  • The "New Trade" Shift: Ambassador Katherine Tai, Executive Director of the Coalition for New Trade, emphasized that trade policy is currently the primary driver of market dynamics, moving away from the traditional "free trade" era toward a model focused on worker protection and national resilience.
  • Tariff Legal Landscape: Following the Supreme Court decision declaring International Emergency Economic Powers Authority (IEEPA) tariffs illegal, the trade environment remains uncertain.
      • Section 301 Investigations: USTR has launched 76 new investigations involving the 60 largest U.S. trading partners.
      • Whipsaw Effect: Importers face a "tariff-rich environment" where specific rates and targeted countries are in constant flux, narrowing the planning horizon for businesses.
  • US-China Relations: A critical meeting between President Trump and President Xi in Beijing is expected to address massive trade imbalances.
      • Market Shift: China’s share of the Port of Los Angeles' business has dropped from 60% in 2018 to 40% today as sourcing migrates to Southeast Asia and Mexico.
      • Agricultural Impact: Soybean exports to China are down 80-90%, forcing the U.S. to seek emerging markets to sustain the farm economy.

Geopolitical Disruptions & Global Logistics

  • Middle East Crisis: The conflict in the Middle East (75 days into the war with Iran) has effectively closed the Strait of Hormuz to normal traffic.
      • Volume Drop: A corridor that typically handles 100+ ships per day is currently seeing only a "handful" of vessels.
      • Energy Ripple Effects: 20% of the world's energy flows through the Strait; the disruption has led to 2,600 flight cancellations/delays in Asia due to surging jet fuel costs.
  • Nearshoring and Diversification: Mexico's manufacturing output has seen double-digit growth over the last eight years.
      • Mexico Investment: Hutchison Ports is expanding to seven terminals across five Mexican ports, though these currently serve specific industrial sectors (automotive/electronics) rather than replacing transcontinental rail loops to the U.S. Midwest.

Port Infrastructure and Strategic Growth

  • Federal Funding Breakthrough: The Port of Los Angeles will receive a record $87 million from the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers.
      • Tax Reform: Historically, the port collected $300M in harbor maintenance taxes but received only $6M back; recent legislative reforms have corrected this imbalance to fund seismic resiliency and navigational safety.
  • Cruise and Community Sector:
      • Cruise Growth: Virgin Voyages and Princess Cruises have launched new West Coast itineraries, following a record-breaking 2025.
      • Fleet Week: The port will host the annual Fleet Week celebration over Memorial Day weekend to honor U.S. sea services.

***

Notable Quotes

"Policy is driving this market like never before. Geopolitics, tariffs, trade frameworks—all of it is reshaping cost, risk, and cargo flow."

— Gene Seroka, Executive Director, Port of Los Angeles

"What distinguishes a trade policy performance from real trade policy is enforcement. What does it matter whether you have rules if you have no way to enforce them? Then the rules just become a fiction."

— Ambassador Katherine Tai, Executive Director, Coalition for New Trade

"Shipping lines are watching every movement very closely, but I haven't spoken to a single executive who wants to make the first move in this setting [the Strait of Hormuz]. Safety and security of ship crews is still the top priority."

— Gene Seroka, Executive Director, Port of Los Angeles

"The free trade era actually incurred a lot of costs that we haven't talked about... we have to completely reimagining free trade to be the kind of trade that creates more winners and fewer losers."

— Ambassador Katherine Tai, Executive Director, Coalition for New Trade

Hello everyone and welcome to our May media briefing from the Port of Los Angeles. I'm Phillip Sanfield, the port's communications director. Each month we offer a pulse check on global trade grounded in data and first-hand insight from America's busiest port. With me in our studio as always is executive director Gene Seroka. Great to see you, Gene. You as well, Phillip, and welcome to everyone joining us today. We appreciate your time and interest. Since we last met, I traveled to Hong Kong, Shanghai, and Guangzhou connecting with customers, ports, and supply chain partners. I also attended the Milken Global Conference here in L.A. engaging with leaders across business, technology, and public policy. One message came through in every meeting and conversation I had. Policy is driving this market like never before. Geopolitics, tariffs, trade frameworks, all of it is reshaping cost, risk, and cargo flow. Today, after we share our latest cargo data, we'll take a deeper dive into those dynamics with a very special guest. Ambassador Katherine Tai will be joining us here in studio 522 at the Port of Los Angeles in just a few minutes. As the former United States Trade Representative and a member of President Joe Biden's cabinet, Ambassador Tai brings a unique perspective on policy decisions shaping today's supply chain. We'll have a frank discussion followed by plenty of time for your questions. Thanks, Gene. I'm looking forward to that conversation. But first, let's get to the numbers. How did the port start Q2 and what's your short-term outlook? Phillip, April came in strong. It was our best month of 2026 and the highest since last August. We handled 891,000 container units. That's up more than five and a half percent from a year ago and 18 percent over the previous month. This ranks as our second best April on record. And what's driving this? Generally speaking, the American consumer. They are still resilient, still spending. And as you can see right here in the data, starting with imports, we processed a robust 460,000 20-foot equivalent units in April. That's up one and a half percent from last year and 21 percent over March. That's significant because last year's numbers were already elevated as importers front-loaded cargo ahead of tariff changes. When I was in Asia a few weeks ago, factories were operating at full capacity moving spring and summer merchandise into the pipeline. That cargo is arriving now here in Los Angeles. The next import wave will be back-to-school products followed by an early holiday inventory. And with U.S. manufacturing holding steady, we're also seeing a consistent flow of parts and components supporting American industry. Now turning to exports, Los Angeles moved out 128,000 container units on par with last year but 13 percent higher than our April five-year average. Exports have been challenged for some time. Although we're beginning to see some recent improvement, there is still plenty of room to grow. Over on the empty side of our business, just over 300,000 units moved out in April, up 10 percent from last year. That equipment is being cycled back to Asia factories for export hubs for moving the next rotation of imports back here to the United States. Now looking at the big picture, four months into the year, Los Angeles dock workers have moved 3.3 million 20-foot equivalent units. That's 2 percent above our five-year average and just 2 percent shy of last year's elevated pace. All that cargo is moved efficiently with no backups or ship delays. This kind of fluidity and consistency speaks to the strength of this port and the teamwork behind it. Our longshore workforce, terminal operators, trucking companies, and rail partners are keeping this gateway running at its best. The foundation matters because the world outside our gates remains unsettled. The conflict in the Middle East continues to cast a long shadow on global trade. Yet here at home, the U.S. economy continues to move forward. Reported was 2 percent GDP growth in Q1. Inflation, although it's higher than many of us would like, is still not runaway. And although jobs have been soft over the past 15 months, last month was just a little bit better and we haven't seen a cratering overall. Retail sales continue to show why consumers are bargain hunting. They're also buying at still near-record paces. These are just a few of the indicators we continue to watch and see as a leading indicator here at the Port of Los Angeles as to how cargo flows may look in the weeks and months ahead. For now, importers remain active helping keep cargo volumes on a steady track heading into the mid-year point. Thank you for that overview, Gene. You mentioned the Middle East. In your view, what will it take for shipping lines to resume normal operations through the Strait of Hormuz? Phillip, simply put, it's going to take confidence, and we're just not there yet. Right now we're seeing in the Strait of Hormuz just a trickle of normal activity. In a corridor that normally handles more than 100 ships a day, we're talking about less than a handful moving through right now. Shipping lines are watching every movement very closely, but I haven't spoken to a single executive who wants to make the first move in this setting. The safety and security of ship crews is still the top priority with 20,000 seafarers still inside the Arabian Gulf on their vessels. And until there's real assurance on that front of safety and security, companies are going to remain cautious. A ceasefire announcement or a few vessels moving under military escort is not enough to restore that confidence. What industry needs is a sustained, proven peace agreement, something that holds over time. Even then, reopening the strait is just the start or the first step. We're now 75 days into this war with Iran. Based on past experience and my time in the Middle East, it will take months to unwind the backlog and get vessels, cargo, and schedules back into a more normal rotation. This is a critical global trade artery. About 20 percent of the world's energy flows through that strait, but also as we've heard, fertilizers, aluminums, plastic, and resin inputs for everyday goods are used. So the ripple effect is being felt not only in fuel markets, but manufacturing inputs and airline operations. Just last week alone in Asia, some 2,600 flights were either cancelled or delayed because of rising fuel costs and tepid supply. To answer your question directly, Phillip, until there's lasting stability and real confidence in safe passage, we're not going to see a return to normal shipping operations in the Middle East nor the attention redirected to other trades soon. Thanks, Gene. Those points take us right into our segment with our special guest. Would you please introduce her? My pleasure, Phillip. We're honored to welcome Ambassador Katherine Tai, who served in President Biden's cabinet as the United States Trade Representative at a truly pivotal moment for global trade. Ambassador Tai previously served as chief trade counsel for the House Ways and Means Committee and held key roles at USTR focused on trade enforcement and China. As a trained trade attorney, she brings a uniquely grounded perspective to both the legal and policy sides of global commerce. Ambassador Tai is now the executive director of the newly established non-profit, the Coalition for New Trade. Ambassador, so great to see you and welcome to the Port of Los Angeles. Thank you so much, Gene. I'm delighted to be here. Tell us a little bit about your organization and what you're doing now. Well, we've just founded the Coalition for New Trade and just like my old job, it's fairly self-explanatory. Given all of the changes, many of which you have just run through in your monthly briefing, it is clear that the world, the world economy, geopolitics, and trade are different today than they were 10 years ago. And they will continue to change. We're in the midst of a huge inflection point and transition or rupture depending on who you're talking to. That means that the way that we approach trade policy making, the way that we approach what we ask for and expect from trade policy, is here upon us and requires us to change our attitudes and our thinking. So the Coalition for New Trade is about building this new coalition around the principles of new trade policies and new trade practicalities. Today, I think everyone is aware of how tariffs are impacting their everyday life and more so than ever before, people are realizing how important ports are to their everyday realities. In the midst of all of this, I think that this is exactly the right time for us to be having these conversations economy-wide with our workers, with our businesses small and large alike, with industry, with the ports, with our agricultural producers, the big ones, but especially the small ones, the family farmers and ranchers. And it's an opportunity to connect with everyone to say, was this trade working for you before? Is this trade working for you today? And in the midst of all of the uncertainty and insecurity, it's an opportunity for us to start putting the pieces together of how we're going to do trade going forward so that more Americans, more people, and more of the world can benefit. That's so important to us and I got a glimpse into how hard that USTR job was serving on one of your advisory committees. We were working across staff and conversations with you over time on just how many countries around the world look to the United States for advice and guidance on trade, and then try to put policies in place that make sense for them and us both. It's important for us here at the port because every four containers create a job. That creates better families and neighborhoods, and supports our city, county, and state here in California. All of this international trade really comes down to the people that move that business on a daily basis, those that produce it as you said, and those who buy it to set it up for us as consumers. On the backdrop of all of that, who are you talking to most now? Who are you trying to build alliances and coalitions with? So the workers are at the center of what we were doing during the Biden administration and they remain front and center for us because workers, especially blue-collar workers, have been the ones that have fared the least well in the trade policies of the last 45 years. As you see President Trump go out with this very pugnacious and disruptive approach to trade and his really strong reliance on tariffs, something that we all should understand is the degree to which those members of our economy who have felt really aggrieved by the era of free trade are drawn and attracted to this message of power and even vengeance when it comes to reordering trade policy and the global economy. So what that tells us is we need an economy and in particular a trade policy that works better for ordinary working people. But that's not enough. We need a coalition to come together to really change the dialogue of how things are working. I think that it's an opportunity for us to re-evaluate in the era of free trade. What was free about it? The free trade actually incurred a lot of costs that we haven't talked about, that we try to paper over. In many ways the moment that we are facing today when it comes to trade policy, geopolitics, and even our domestic politics is a result of sweeping inconvenient realities under the rug for too long. So what that tells me as a trade policy expert is that we have to completely reimagine free trade to be the kind of trade that creates more winners, fewer losers, and needs to be completely reorganized. A rules-based trade approach only makes sense if it protects the things that are most sacrosanct, right? Growth in the American economy, iconic brands, and those jobs associated with them. But being so reliant on international trade, it cannot just be a blanket approach one way or the other, correct? That's completely correct. And I would say rules-based trade is still a value worth defending. The issue is what is in those rules and whether or not those rules are still fit for purpose today. I think it's pretty clear that those rules have really run their course. We need new rules that will safeguard and to your point protect those people in our economy, those parts of this vibrant ecosystem that are critical to our success as a nation and to thriving opportunities for our people. So with all of that and we've talked about inflation and trade deals and the imbalance of trade that some have said lead to America last if you will, where do we look next? We've got a war going on in Iran that's spiked up energy costs and inputs for, think of plastic bags, prices are going through the roof, etc. So what should we be looking at next and what are you focused on for the future of trade? I think what I was listening to in your briefing about the energy trade being affected by the Strait of Hormuz and the constriction and the dynamics around war and conflict, right? There's energy, fertilizer, the plastics piece. If you think about it through the lens of agriculture, food security, there's the fertilizer as an input into production and harvesting of food. There's also the plastics that go into so much of the packaging of the distribution of food in our system and around the world. What I'm looking to next is everybody has assumed for a long time that prosperity would lead to peace. I think it's fair to say that today our world is less peaceful even though in the macro it's extremely prosperous. What I'm focused on is what happens when you don't have the peace and I think that the prospects for prosperity become much more dim. So there's the conflict in Iran and in the Middle East that is really critical. There's also the conversation that President Trump is about to have with President Xi on this state visit to China that is going to be really important. How the two largest economies in the world deal with each other, especially the world's largest producer and the world's largest consumer, is going to set the stage for what's possible for, and I don't think this is hyperbole, for everybody alive today. Couldn't agree with you more. And as we head into those meetings this week in Beijing, much of the shipping community and others who follow international trade are looking at the pending expiry of the temporary tariffs off the Supreme Court's decision on the IEEPA tariffs, the International Emergency Economic Powers Authority, and what that could mean next. So that uncertainty still lingers for the small business person who buys products from overseas and has to figure out does it pencil or not. What do you see happening on that landscape with a couple of moving parts all at once? So the moment the IEEPA tariffs, which were laid down in early 2025, were declared illegal by the Supreme Court, that was at the end of February. The administration moved out very quickly. I think that they were disappointed, in the case of President Trump extremely disappointed, but not surprised. And so the administration had a plan in place. They rolled out these tariffs under a different statutory authority, Section 122. These tariffs on their face of the statutory authority can last for 150 days until the end of July unless reauthorized by Congress, which with this Congress is probably unlikely. Those have been declared illegal by a lower court just recently. And so what do we expect from that? We expect those court proceedings to continue. The administration has already appealed that court decision and it will take some time to get through. In the meantime, I want people to be focused on what USTR and the Department of Commerce are doing. USTR in the middle of March, so less than two weeks after the Supreme Court decision on IEEPA, rolled out 76 new investigations under Section 301 of the Trade Act of 1974. Those 76 investigations are focused on 60 different trading partners of the United States and I would say they are the 60 largest trading partners of the U.S. Those investigations have six months to run but they don't need to run for the full six months. I would expect outcomes of those investigations to be announced in time for new potential tariff actions to be deployed as the 122 tariffs run their course. So what does this all mean for people in the trade and people who rely on imports and exports to power their economies? I think what this means is that there will be tariffs for the foreseeable future. But it is not entirely clear which tariffs directed at which countries at which rates. And so that's to say tariff-rich environments will continue and the uncertainty around how those tariffs are deployed will also continue. With the refunds that are now being rolled out under IEEPA, what occurs to me is the incredible amount of administrative and bureaucratic headache and the heyday for lawyers and consultants in the middle of all of this. It's a deeply disruptive time, but I think it's also a really good opportunity even if it's uncomfortable and hard for everyone that is directly involved in trade to develop their agility and their resilience. And we've talked a lot about that over the years, whether it was COVID standing back up to an economy that was just roaring especially based on consumer buying patterns, and now all of this uncertainty that started with the campaign trail back in the summer of 2024 leading up to now and more lever points that are going to be going forward. It's a lot even for the most seasoned professional to try to keep a steady hand on the tiller when you've got all these different moving parts. Here at the Port of Los Angeles, we have over 200,000 companies that ship through this port on an annual basis. The, you touched on it earlier, the American farmer has been really put into a spotlight that they may not have asked for. We only consume about a third of all the crops that we harvest. The balance two-thirds go to renewable energy and to emerging markets. That's not happening. Soybean trade to China was down 90 percent last year and down 80 percent through this port. Do you think we'll start to see any more focus on the American farmer on those crops that are so important not only to the United States but the world because they're high quality? I hope so. I would say though that the farmers really end up getting the short end of the stick during Trump times, although President Trump I think has a tremendous amount of support from rural America, from farming America. I think that President Trump really feels like he has a lot of political capital that he can spend from that kind of support. The number of dollars that go out during Trump times, that's in the first Trump administration and then now in the first half of this Trump administration, to bail out farmers who are impacted by the Trump trade policies is really mind-boggling when you have the opportunity to talk to some of these farmers. What you discover is that first of all they are extremely savvy business people and second of all that they are extremely proud. They don't like taking handouts to get them through periods of time. What matters to them is their market opportunity, their relationship with their customers, and their ability to produce high quality product. And so as a farm bill is moving its way through the Congress, it's never easy. I think in particular this year as we're looking at that legislation move along, we are starting to realize how all aspects of our economy, including the farm economy, including farm trade, require new thinking and a renewed approach. The way that we have been doing things has been really challenged by this administration which has embraced its role as a vector of change itself. I think that means that again new coalitions need to be formed to figure out what are the different policy pieces including trade but not limited to trade have to be put together for a more resilient and enduring vibrant economy for America and for America's place in the world. And right here at home that impacts us too because if exports start to slow as we've seen them over the past 15-16 months and imports continue at a decent pace, you've got a greater imbalance of trade on the container business than we've seen before. What it means is the importer pays now for that empty return of a container back to Asia for the next round of imports because the economies of scale or the round trip economics as we call the industry start to get a little bit off-balance. And you've got containers that have to get back. The imports are paying okay, but if you have a little more balance of trade, if you have those ag products going out at a higher rate of speed, prices come down on both sides which are then to the benefit of the American consumer. I think that that's a piece of wisdom that is really important. That's a piece of real world wisdom that's important for policy makers in Washington to hear. More on that in a moment. You mentioned that this week President Xi Jinping from China and President Donald Trump of the United States will meet in Beijing. This was put off a little bit in time from an earlier scheduled meeting. What does success look like in this round of discussions for the United States? So when these talks were initially proposed and previewed for all of us in the public, I thought this is a really important moment for the U.S.-China trade relationship which has been going through a lot, which is marked by significant imbalance. Right? The Chinese produce more than they can consume themselves and in fact they are not consuming enough at home. The big deal economists look at the Chinese economy and they say the Chinese economy would be stronger if they had stronger consumption. One of the things that you can interpret President Trump as saying about the American economy is that we consume too much. Now I'm taking a little bit of interpretive liberty when President Trump says, you know, children in America have too many dolls and they could do with fewer dolls. I'm interpreting that to be a comment on the consumer economy, the power of the consumer economy in the United States and maybe the United States could consume less on the macro level and start producing more. You see these two elements of imbalance in these two large economies and how they relate to each other. I've been hopeful that in high level talks between Washington and Beijing that exactly these dynamics might come onto the table. They're not easy to solve but they're going to be impossible to solve without the two sides coming together. Given that the initial talks when they were proposed were delayed because of the war with Iran, which is not wrapped up, we've all noticed, my concern is that when these two leaders come together that the trade and economic relationship conversation slips further into the background and that these critical and actually urgent conversations that need to be had between the two sides don't get had. What does success look like for China in these meetings if that postulate is true? I mean I think that it's, if I were to try to put myself in the shoes of the policy makers in Beijing, I think that they have to be regarding the U.S. as the vector of change and chaos that I think we present to all of the rest of the world. And so for the folks in Beijing, I think it's about holding onto as much of the status quo as possible. The status quo of the free trade era, of WTO membership, has worked very well for China. And so I see the Chinese as being defenders of a system that needs to change. And the longer that they can hold onto that status quo, the more successful they will be. We saw that last year in a couple of ways. When the U.S. tariffs, which were erratic at best and a real whipsaw effect to most of us, we saw that China really ramped up their exports to other trading partners. So much so that by the end of the year, exports from China to everywhere else around the world but the U.S. were up 21 percent. And trade with the United States, imports to the U.S. were down about 9 percent. So they're looking for other customers in different places and trying to make up that supply capacity that they have by selling to these other countries. At the same time, we now see that the exports from the U.S. have dwindled, the prices on imports have grown, coffee and bananas are up 10 percent year over year, and we're not going to produce either. And on that balance of consuming economy versus manufacturing and actually selling products on the world stage, according to the National Association of Manufacturers, we have about 400,000 manufacturing jobs open in this country today. How do you square all of that in the midst of the most high profile trade negotiations since you were meeting along with President Biden and Xi Jinping on the sidelines of APEC in Northern California? I think what all of this is prompting is the hunger on the part of Americans in the American economy, but also for the people around the world in all of their separate economies, for a coherence in terms of policy and messaging from Washington. Something that makes sense that people can follow that they can plan their businesses on. That's right. Even if you don't agree, having that clarity in terms of vision and messaging and signaling allows for people to get with the program because at the end of the day, everyone wants to make their economy work whether that's their household economy, whether that's their company economy, whether that's their workforce economy. And I think that that is the piece that in this time of change I feel like I miss the most, which is change for the sake of change leaves a lot wanting. Change that is led by a particular vision, especially a positive vision, is I think something that we all really hunger for. Sure, and what I hear from businesses almost every day, every meeting, is just tell me the ground rules and I'll get to work. But it's all these changing goalposts and whipsaw effects of announcements and things of that nature because the folks not just in the C-suite, not just the executives, but the operations people who we work with every day on moving cargo in and out of here, all tell me that their planning horizon is so narrow because they don't know what's going to happen next. What's the next shoe to drop? I think that that's what is the most important for the companies that can weather this period of whipsaw. I've been told that when they've felt like sharing with me that they've kind of priced that in for these few years. So the question for them is what's going to come after. If they're going to be looking for guideposts and North stars as they're hunkered down to survive these few years, what's the longer term vision? And I think that that's where conversations right now are most important for us to be planting seeds for. And I know that on so many levels, folks around the world look to the United States, look to the leadership in Washington to help navigate through tough times, no matter what they may be, right? Trade, policy, climate change, and getting along with each other, neighbors and borders that shift. But at this juncture, at least my witness is when I travel overseas, folks do a double-take and want to make sure that we're still the same people, we still want to trade, we still want to do business here at the Port of Los Angeles. Kind of a tough question, but when you were in the chair, you're creating trade opportunities, negotiating trade deals. Enforcement always comes up. We heard just last fall China's going to buy a ton of soybeans from us. We're getting ready to go and all my buddies in the Midwest are gearing up. We really haven't seen that. So what about the enforcement mechanism in trying to cobble together these trade deals to make sure that milestones are hit and commitments are lived up to? I think that the trade enforcement piece is so important. What does it matter whether you have rules if your system is rules-based, if you have no way to enforce them? Then the rules just become a fiction, right? At best they develop a certain kind of code of behavior, but without enforcement mechanisms, there's no meaning to the rules. I think this is a great question especially here where we're sitting at the Port of L.A. because there's a lot of enforcement that has to happen at the border. There's a larger question also of the deals that this administration is striking, whether it is the phase one agreement with China back from 2020 or a lot of these, what are they calling them, agreements on reciprocal trade that are being negotiated around these extra tariffs from 2025. There's a real question about their meaning and their durability which go to their enforceability. I'll say the other thing, the traditional tool of enforcement at the state-to-state level beyond the inspection capacity and the ability to stop things at the border here at the ports and along the border, the big enforcement tool that countries wield has traditionally been tariffs. In an environment where tariffs are already so frequent, so volatile, the question is whether or not relying on that tool for enforcement still has meaning. If everybody's used to all these tariffs flying around and you're threatening more tariffs because of lack of enforcement, I think that they just feel like more tariffs and I think that we see that right now in the dynamics with the European Union. Yeah, and this is tough. Just if I can make an analogous statement. As a sales guy in this industry when I was a lot younger, I went out and signed a contract with an importer and exporter for 100 containers. I had to show my boss at the end of the year we got those 100 containers. You don't hear a lot of that dialogue with all of this discussion around trade and tariffs and enforcement and making good and all that kind of thing. We're not having that report back as we would call it, right? And saying look, we made this deal, here's where we hit, we're at this mile marker, now we're going to the next level. Gene, I would say that the thing that distinguishes a trade policy performance from real trade policy is enforcement. Agreed. Ambassador so good, we could go on for a long time but I know that we have a bunch of questions from the media. Phillip, back over to you. Thank you both. Something that we need to know about the USMCA is that it was authorized as a limited-time agreement for only 16 years. Thank you, Ambassador. Gene, this question is for you. It's from Bill Mongelluzzo of the Journal of Commerce. Red Hat Summit 2026 Day 1 Keynote - The next platform is choice

Red Hat(164.0K)·95:15·10.3K views·2026-05-12
Matt HicksPresident and CEO, Red Hat
TL;DRAt Red Hat Summit 2026, Red Hat launched its "metal-to-agents" open-source AI platform to help enterprises bridge the gap between rising IT complexity and flat budgets.
By unifying virtual machines, containers, and AI workloads, Red Hat enables organizations to escape...

TL;DR

At Red Hat Summit 2026, Red Hat launched its "metal-to-agents" open-source AI platform to help enterprises bridge the gap between rising IT complexity and flat budgets. By unifying virtual machines, containers, and AI workloads, Red Hat enables organizations to escape virtualization price crises, control token economics, and maintain digital sovereignty.

1. Navigating the Virtualization Cost Crisis and Hybrid Infrastructure Consolidation

Enterprise IT is facing a critical chasm: managing legacy architectures while simultaneously running modern AI initiatives on flat budgets.

  • The Virtualization Crisis: Enterprises are facing potential 3x to 5x cost increases for legacy hypervisor footprints.
  • Unified Operations via OpenShift: Red Hat OpenShift provides a single platform to run and manage virtual machines (VMs), containers, and AI workloads side-by-side with a consistent security posture.
    • Rapid Adoption: The number of VMs running on OpenShift Virtualization grew by 417% over the past year. Red Hat assessed over 1.1 million VMs and successfully migrated nearly 400,000.
  • Motorola Solutions Case Study: Senior Director Anudeep Muttavarapu explained how the company modernized its mission-critical first-responder network.
    • Consolidating Silos: Motorola shifted from managing separate VM and container environments to a single, Kubernetes-based hybrid deployment fabric spanning on-prem (for near-zero latency) and cloud (for AI-powered translation and summarization).
    • Invisible Infrastructure: Muttavarapu stated their ultimate goal is to make infrastructure "like oxygen—always there, always reliable," ensuring dispatchers and emergency responders never have to think about server uptime.

2. Enabling Digital and AI Sovereignty in Regulated Environments

Digital sovereignty has moved from the legal department to the architecture review board due to expanding global regulations, including the EU AI Act, NIS2, DORA, and GDPR.

  • Defining AI Sovereignty: Red Hat outlines three critical pillars for true enterprise autonomy:
    • Model Sovereignty: The flexibility to choose, change, and run models anywhere.
    • Data Sovereignty: Verifiable assurance that training and fine-tuning data never leaves the organization's control or geographic jurisdiction.
    • Outcome Sovereignty: The ability to audit, trace, and override AI-generated decisions.
  • New Sovereign Offerings:
    • Compliance & Landing Zones: Enhanced compliance frameworks for European regulations and a cross-platform installer to enforce operational guardrails.
    • In-Region Content Delivery: A European Content Delivery Network (CDN) allowing users to download Red Hat Enterprise Linux (RHEL) within the EU, with expansion to other products by the end of 2026.
    • Sovereign Support & Core: Confirmed Sovereign Support in the EU delivered by vetted, in-region engineers. Additionally, IBM launched *IBM Sovereign Core*, built on top of Red Hat platforms to verify sovereign controls.
  • Core42 Case Study: Raghu Chakravarthi, EVP of Engineering, detailed UAE-based Core42's role as a sovereign cloud operator.
    • Technological Autonomy: Core42 is the first to run OpenStack 18.1 on OpenShift, unifying VM and container operations across thousands of production servers to support highly classified top-secret workloads.
    • Global Expansion: Core42 is expanding its sovereign model globally, operating an AI factory in the US with over 100 megawatts of capacity and deploying Europe’s largest AI compute cluster in Italy.
  • EUROCONTROL and Telenor:
    • EUROCONTROL: Is refactoring its 30-year-old core IT backbone into a modern API architecture on Red Hat, reducing post-operational flight data analysis wait times from three days to real-time.
    • Telenor: Created Norway’s first sovereign and secure AI cloud service (the Telenor AI factory) running localized language models on Red Hat and NVIDIA infrastructure.

3. Mastering Token Economics with the "Metal-to-Agents" Stack

With generative AI consumption skyrocketing over 500% annually, enterprises must shift from being simple token consumers to token providers by hosting their own optimized infrastructure.

  • The Red Hat AI Enterprise Stack: A fully integrated, open, "metal-to-agents" stack running on any cloud and supporting accelerators from NVIDIA, AMD, and Intel.
    • Inference Layer (vLLM and LLM-d): Red Hat is a leading contributor to vLLM. It paired vLLM with its open-source distributed inference framework, LLM-d, which delivers 3x more output tokens and 10x faster time-to-first-token.
    • Model Services & AI Gateway: The new Red Hat AI Gateway acts as a single control plane to handle rate limiting, token quotas, and credential access across over 700 validated model variations.
    • Agent Services & AgentOps: Supports a "Bring Your Own Agents" strategy, providing identity verification, lifecycle management, Model Context Protocol (MCP) tool-calling, and tracing to eliminate agent sprawl.
  • Internal Optimization at Red Hat: Red Hat transitioned its own internal agentic systems from expensive frontier models to open-weights models running on its own infrastructure.
    • Result: 85% of calls on Red Hat’s deep research agent system now run on open-source, open-weights models (including IBM Granite and NVIDIA Nemotron), resulting in lower unit costs and improved accuracy.
  • BNP Paribas Success: The European banking leader scaled its AI factory using Red Hat AI, deploying over 1,000 use cases and generating nearly $600 million in additional business value while reducing GPU provisioning from weeks to minutes.

4. Hardware Co-Design and Autonomous Networks at the Edge

Realizing the benefits of agentic AI requires tight integration with hardware partners and hyper-scale edge orchestration.

  • NVIDIA Extreme Co-Design: Justin Boitano discussed the collaboration behind the Red Hat AI factory with NVIDIA.
    • Silicon Optimization: Red Hat announced GA support for NVIDIA Grace Blackwell and a roadmap for day-zero support of the upcoming Vera Rubin architecture.
    • OpenShell Project: Red Hat and NVIDIA are co-engineering OpenShell, an open-source, secure agent governance layer that provides sandboxed, zero-trust environments for self-evolving agentic workflows.
  • Verizon's Autonomous Edge Networks: Praveen Atreya, VP of Technology, detailed Verizon's ten-year journey with the Verizon Cloud Platform (VCP).
    • Massive Scale: Verizon is using OpenShift AI to run artificial intelligence across 100,000 edge nodes.
    • Self-Driving Networks: In 2025, Verizon executed millions of network changes in a closed-loop, automated mode with no human in the loop. Utilizing agentic AI, the company is building proactive triage loops to resolve individual customer experience degradations in near real-time before the customer reports an issue.

Notable Quotes

"The chasm between the complexity of your environment and the resources you have to work with it, and the speed at which your business needs to compete—that is the real story of enterprise IT right now, not just AI." — Matt Hicks, President and CEO, Red Hat
"Success for us means infrastructure and technology becomes invisible. It should be like oxygen: always there, always reliable, and never something a police officer or a firefighter has to think about." — Anudeep Muttavarapu, Senior Director of Core Platforms and Services, Motorola Solutions
"To win token economics, you have to move from being just a token consumer to an actual token provider." — Chris Wright, SVP and Chief Technology Officer, Red Hat
"The only point you may have missed is we are living through the world's biggest industrial revolution as we build out AI... the name of the game is you got to convert power into tokens, and these tokens are going to be the intelligence that powers your business." — Justin Boitano, NVIDIA
We are standing on the shoulders of visionaries who dreamed that we would be where we are today. ...is just about dreaming of a better world. It's recognizing where emerging technologies can solve global human challenges. Cloud would not be here without Linux and open source software. We've changed the world. We are watching the impact and potential of open source that played out all those years ago, now playing out with AI. Welcome to Red Hat Summit 2026. Please welcome Red Hat President and Chief Executive Officer, Matt Hicks. Good morning. Look at this crowd. Every one of you is responsible for something that cannot break, cannot go down, and cannot be wrong. Whether it's millions of financial transactions being processed every hour, or healthcare networks that physicians depend on for life-affecting decisions, or the millions of consumers served by telecommunications networks—that's just a normal week for the people in this room. And here's what I think about a lot: the outcomes get all the attention—the deployment measures, the SLAs, the uptime measurements. What doesn't get seen is the craft behind them that makes it possible. The person that designed the architecture from scratch. The person that migrated hundreds of virtual machines, invisibly to the business. The person that, at 3 AM in the morning, figures out the cause of an outage that wasn't in the runbook. That takes real expertise, real creativity, and yet, most of the world never sees it. I see it, and I think it's important to address that before we talk about anything else today. Now, a question for you. Hands up, and be honest with me on this one. How many of you are managing an environment today that is more complex than what you were managing two years ago? Put your hands up. Okay, now keep your hands up if your budget has grown at the same rate as that complexity. Yeah, there aren't a lot of hands up. Now think about, in addition to this: how many of you are being asked to run an AI initiative while also managing a legacy system that is absolutely critical to your business? So, these constraints, these pressures—the new initiative pressure, the organizational friction, the legacy constraints, all happening at once—that is the daily reality for the people in this room. That combination is the villain in the room. Think about your reality: your virtualization costs are up significantly, your regulatory environment is expanding beyond your compliance team's ability to keep up, and your board is probably pushing for ROI on AI projects at this point that might not match your enterprise reality. And yet, your people, your talented, hard-working people are being asked to modernize, and secure, and operate, and innovate on these same timelines, with the same budget. That gap—that chasm between the complexity of your environment, the resources you have to work with, and the speed at which your business needs to compete—that is the real story of enterprise IT right now, not just AI. And if we're not talking about that chasm, then we're not talking about your reality. Now, I've actually seen these pressures before, which lead to a question at practically every customer that I talk to: "What do I build on?" Not what vendor, not what cloud, not what model. What is the foundation that lets me compete at the speed that my business needs while not breaking what absolutely cannot be broken in my business? And I've seen this before, twice actually. Both times, it happened in ways that fundamentally reshaped the industry. The first time was in the shift from proprietary hardware and proprietary operating systems to open infrastructure. To that question, the "What do I build on?" question, the answer turned out to be Linux. Red Hat brought Linux to the enterprise with Red Hat Enterprise Linux, and RHEL became a standard in practically every industry. Everything that followed was built on Linux: cloud, IoT, mobile. Think about today—every AI model, every AI agent runs on Linux. That foundation that we built together is now the reality of what everything we depend on today is running on. Now, the second time this came up was in the shift to cloud-native: containers, Kubernetes, and microservices. At Red Hat, we didn't invent Docker; we didn't invent Kubernetes. We brought Kubernetes to the enterprise through OpenShift, and now that is a standard that thousands of customers depend on for their hybrid cloud. We are at the third inflection point right now with AI, and Red Hat will bring AI to the enterprise to make that an open standard. And I think the pattern holds from the last two. The organizations that will have the most success in this environment are not necessarily going to be the ones that spend the most, or the ones that move the fastest. They will be the ones that build on the right foundation. Virtual machines, containers, and AI agents are not competing with each other—they are converging. And the answer to this is not going to be one cloud, one vendor, or one model. It will be the right platform with a broad ecosystem that supports it. I'm biased, but I think the organizations that will have the most success will pick a platform with an open foundation below it. Now, speaking of AI, let's take a minute and talk about our AI journey at Red Hat, because we're going through the same inflection point at the same time as all of you. As you know, Red Hat is an open source company, so what we build we share. That is how open source works. At this point, we have deployed generative AI to every organization in the company. Each organization is looking for ways to either drive more efficiency for them or create more value for you, the customers. For example, you might have heard about or used Ask Red Hat, which is our interactive chatbot. It runs on our customer support portal. Now, this chatbot has been trained on over two decades of support information, our knowledge base, and support capabilities within Red Hat. This is a case where, hopefully, there is more value for you when you're operating in the known of support, but it is also more efficient for us and saves us millions of dollars a year. Now, in this journey, we've had plenty of successes. We have also had plenty of failures. That's easy to do when the technology is changing practically weekly now, but those learnings are going actively back into the platforms that we provide you. Everything you're going to hear about at Summit—our confidence, our belief that we can be the right foundation for your journey—is not just a market position. It's what we are actually doing in Red Hat. Now, about a year ago, we moved from the world of chatbots to building AI agents. Specifically, we wanted to build an agentic system that could go through a massive body of internal knowledge and return something genuinely useful—not a summary, but an actual answer. And we started where many of you probably start: with frontier models, the best, most capable models that we could work with. We wanted to understand what was possible before we decided how to build this right. And it worked. After we had about ten agents working together, it was clear that this system worked and would continue to work. So we did what was very natural to Red Hat: we started to optimize. We went through layer by layer, figuring out which of these models could be replaced by open weight models running on vLLM on infrastructure we control. First, we actually rebuilt the document search capability. We found out that small models excel in correctly formatted documents to find the right answers, so we moved document search. Next came hallucination detection. Our ten initial agents had now scaled to almost 200, and you will deal with hallucinations. Small models were an excellent way for us to do that double-checking and hold hallucinations at bay. We moved from hallucinations to safety management, controlling what we would answer and what would come in. Small models were incredible there. Lastly, we moved to planning—the most complicated step where an agent has to look at all the other agents it could talk to and figure out what questions to ask to get that genuinely useful answer. Layer by layer, we went through the entire application to figure out what we could switch from the frontier models to open source models running on our infrastructure and technology. Today, 85% of the calls running on our deep research agent system call open source, open weight models running on Red Hat infrastructure. And here's what surprised us: of course, this was more efficient, but our results actually got better. And it wasn't because frontier models weren't the right place to start, but when you own the model and you own the infrastructure, you can optimize in a way that a frontier model that is general purpose could never anticipate. The heart of our system today is running on Nemotron Super, Nemotron Nano, and IBM Granite. This is all running on Red Hat infrastructure, and now we are at an efficiency point—from a cost, a unit cost perspective—that we are able to start rolling this out to the entire company. What we built is not a demo. This wasn't a pilot. This is fundamentally changing how we operate at Red Hat. And our learnings go directly back into the platforms that we provide for you all. It needs to support virtual machines for your legacy and mission-critical applications. It needs to support containers and cloud-native applications for the workloads that your developers are building today and for tomorrow. And for AI, as you heard from Matt, it needs to support a future where you'll have GPU-intensive, inference-heavy workloads. These are three fundamentally different workload types. Now, most platforms will ask you to independently manage them in three different ways, with three different operational models and three different cost structures. We feel that's an unsustainable ask for any team. What if there was one platform with one operational model and one consistent security posture? That's exactly what OpenShift delivers, and it's different from every other platform in the market today. Virtual machines, containers, and AI workloads are all running side-by-side, managed the same way, operated the same way, and secured the same way. Providing that platform, though, is just one part of the answer. As you all well know, migrating that virtualization environment is not a weekend project. It's complex, touches hundreds of applications, and carries real business risk. We know this because we have done this with some of our largest customers at scale, and so have our partners. We know something else, too. The hardest part of that work is not the technology. It's never been the technology. It's the people—the engineers who have to keep the old environment running at full availability while simultaneously building the new one. All of this must be done without additional headcount and without missing a single SLA in the process. Now, that is a big ask, and it's exactly the kind of ask that gets made without anyone in finance fully understanding what it requires. So, we've built migration assessment tooling that gives you complete visibility into your application estate before you move even a single workload. In the last year, we assessed over 1.1 million virtual machines. We established a phased migration approach that lets you move at your own pace, with full fallback options at every stage. In the last year, we migrated nearly 400,000 virtual machines. The number of VMs running on OpenShift Virtualization grew by 417% in just the last year. Simply put, clusters running VMs on OpenShift: way up; customers running VMs on OpenShift: way up. Now, to achieve the scale that you need, we invested deeply in the systems integrator ecosystem that will actually be in the room when this work happens. So, when you think about this holistically, this isn't a migration from one VM platform to another. It's a migration, really, from a virtualization strategy to a modernization strategy. Now, I know what some of you are thinking right now: why not just go all-in with a hyperscaler? Just move it all to the cloud. That's a fair question. Here are three things that hyperscaler-native services fundamentally cannot give you. First: consistency. When you build on hyperscaler-native services, you'll get a different operational model on every cloud—different tooling, a different security model, and different networking primitives. OpenShift gives you one operational model that works the same way everywhere: on your private infrastructure, on AWS, on Azure, on Google Cloud, on the edge, and even in disconnected and regulated environments. Second: independence. When you standardize deeply on hyperscaler-native services, you trade negotiating leverage for convenience. That's antithetical to the Red Hat business model. It's built on your flexibility, not your lock-in. We started talking about open hybrid cloud ten years ago. It is foundational to who we are. Third: regulated reality. For many organizations in this room—financial services, healthcare, government, telecommunications—100% public cloud isn't possible due to legal constraints. Hybrid, then, is not really a compromise for these industries. It is the only viable strategy. Love the vision, love the ambition of Core42, Raghu, and the team. Now, data sovereignty is the foundation, but AI sovereignty, as you heard from Raghu, is the next evolution of the same challenge. And it is where the stakes are significantly higher. Let me define AI sovereignty precisely, because I think it's a term that's getting used loosely. There are three things you must own to have genuine AI sovereignty in your organization. One: model sovereignty—the ability to choose which AI models run in your environment, the ability to change them as the market evolves (and we're all seeing that it will evolve dramatically), and the ability to run them anywhere based on what your business, your data, and your regulators require. Two: data sovereignty—the peace of mind that the data you use to train, fine-tune, and run your AI models never leaves your control, that it stays in the jurisdictions your regulators require, that it's not used to train anyone else's models without your explicit consent, and that you know precisely and verifiably where it lives and who has access to it at every moment. Three: outcome sovereignty—the ability to audit every significant AI-generated decision, to explain it, to trace it back to the data and model that produced it, and if necessary, to override it. AI sovereignty isn't a theoretical conversation. Customers are actively addressing this today. Telenor was founded in 1855, and it began its journey as a state-operated telegraph service in Norway. Now, before we dive into the platform, let's just reflect on the business reality that makes this platform so important. Right now, most enterprise AI runs like this: you call a frontier model API, and you pay for the tokens you send and the tokens that it generates for you. It's super easy to start, they are powerful models, and for some workloads, genuinely just the right answer. But here's the dynamic that's worth watching closely. Per-token pricing is coming down 75 to 90% a year. That's the good news. Token consumption, however, is skyrocketing, increasing over 500% a year in some cases. And so, with these new reasoning models that are generating even more tokens as they think, consuming 10 to 20 times more tokens than standard models, and with AI agents, you'll have to expect another 5x multiple. This is because an AI agent reasons—it plans, as you heard from Matt, calls tools, iterates, loops, and is connected to other agents in a workflow. So to win token economics, you have to move from being just a token consumer to an actual token provider. And we're working with organizations around the world to enable just that. Organizations that own part of their own inference infrastructure, that run self-hosted models optimized for their business, and have real choice over which model to use for which job—those are the organizations that will emerge as the winners. All right, so let's dive into the platform. Now, remember what Matt described earlier: that journey from frontier models to open source models hosted internally, which ran on this platform, Red Hat AI Enterprise. It's the platform that really made it possible. At the foundation, Red Hat AI Enterprise runs on any accelerator and any cloud, giving our customers choice and control over where they deliver AI capabilities for their business. This includes accelerators from NVIDIA, AMD, Intel, and from the cloud providers' custom silicon—like in AWS, Google Cloud, Azure, and whatever comes next across public, private, and edge environments. That's the foundation. Now let's go through the Red Hat Enterprise AI stack that makes "metal to agents" possible. It begins at this AI infrastructure layer. Now, this is where the IT operations, SRE, and platform engineering teams live. Today, AI runs on Linux and is scaled with Kubernetes. RHEL provides this Linux foundation, the trusted and reliable operating system that's built with the security needs of both current and future workloads in mind. So we protect the foundation of your AI system. Everything above this really depends on getting this layer right. Red Hat OpenShift is our unified Kubernetes platform for virtual machines, for containers, and for AI workloads. Now, network isolation can control what an agent can reach, and GPU sharing across workloads means your most expensive resources are not locked to a single team, or worse, just sitting idle between inference calls. Next are inference services. This is where your token economics layer lives, where performance, scalability, efficiency, and cost—this is where it all comes together. Inference is the layer that most directly determines whether your AI strategy succeeds at scale. If you get inference wrong, your customers aren't happy, your data science teams aren't happy, your AI agents aren't happy, and your CFO is definitely not happy. So we deliver world-class inference services through vLLM, the open standard for inference serving. And Red Hat is super proud to be the leading contributor to the vLLM project. Now, vLLM is complemented by LLM-d, our open source distributed inference framework. We built LLM-d because AI workloads aren't like typical cloud-native workloads. Enterprise AI workloads often don't fit on just a single GPU. They're stateful, they fundamentally rely on KV cache, and they vary dramatically from request to request. So LLM-d intelligently routes and schedules requests across your environment, optimizing how models execute, how GPU resources are used, and how latency is managed. Remember those token economics I mentioned? In just one year, the performance improvements with LLM-d are three times more output tokens and 10x faster time to first token. Now, there's a great paper that was recently published by Red Hat and Tesla along with the open source community that has all the details. Next up, we've got model services. Now, this is where choice is critical given all the changes happening with frontier models, with open-weight models, with domain-specific models, and even small language models. The starting point for many companies is delivering models-as-a-service to their internal application and data science teams. Red Hat AI Enterprise just makes that easy. When your AI teams need to add or remove or swap a model, you do it in just one place. Your teams choose the models they want, and token quotas, credentialed access, request priorities, and rate limiting—all of it is delivered through our AI gateway. It's one control plane for every model interaction across your environment. Now, the reality is that companies will use many different models to deliver the AI capabilities that their business needs. And our vLLM team has deep expertise with a wide variety of models. In fact, almost every major new model update is optimized for vLLM right on day zero. So to bring all that innovation and optimization to our customers, the "validated models by Red Hat" program has more than 20 collections of models and nearly 700 variations of models that have all been validated on vLLM and then optimized for speed and efficiency. So this should give you a sense of our commitment when we say that Red Hat AI supports any model. Computer use in Codex

OpenAI(1.9M)·11:26·84.9K views·2026-05-12
Ari WeinsteinOpenAI
TL;DRCodex now features "computer use" capabilities that allow it to interact with local desktop applications via an independent cursor, enabling the agent to complete complex tasks in the background without interrupting the user's workflow.
Non-Invasive Execution: Unlike...

TL;DR

Codex now features "computer use" capabilities that allow it to interact with local desktop applications via an independent cursor, enabling the agent to complete complex tasks in the background without interrupting the user's workflow.

Background Multitasking and User Experience

  • Non-Invasive Execution: Unlike previous "computer use" implementations that take over the entire screen, Codex operates with its own independent cursor.
      • This allows users to continue working on other tasks while the agent clicks, types, and navigates through local applications in the background.
  • Visual Intuition: OpenAI designed the agent's cursor movement to feel "natural and whimsical," with the arrow turning in the direction of motion to help users visually track what the agent is doing.
  • Multi-App Orchestration: Codex can drive multiple applications simultaneously.
      • Example: In a single session, Ari Weinstein demonstrated Codex downloading a macOS virtual machine in UTM, playing music in Spotify, and adding a tax-related task to the Reminders app.

Technical Framework: Accessibility and Multimodal Integration

  • Beyond Screenshots: Traditional agents rely solely on screenshots and coordinate-based clicking. Codex enhances this by leveraging the macOS Accessibility Framework.
      • Deep Context: By pulling textual data from the accessibility layer, Codex can identify the role of UI elements and "see" content that is currently scrolled off-screen.
  • Model Versatility:
      • Multimodal Models: Use visual data to understand layouts and perform tasks by coordinate.
      • Codex Spark: A faster, non-multimodal model that relies on accessibility data to perform tasks at "superhuman" speeds, often faster than a human could physically move a mouse.
  • Streamlined Development: OpenAI has moved away from training dedicated models for computer use, instead integrating these capabilities into the mainline GPT models available via API.

Security and Granular Permissions

  • App-by-App Authorization: To ensure user trust, Codex does not have blanket access to the file system or screen.
      • Users must explicitly "Allow" Codex to interact with each specific application the first time it is used.
  • Privacy Isolation: If Codex is granted permission to use a productivity app, it remains unable to see or interact with sensitive data in other open applications (e.g., private messages or financial data) that have not been authorized.
  • Onboarding Flow: The setup process is integrated into the macOS settings, using a simple drag-and-drop interface to authorize the agent within the system framework.

The Future of "Superhuman" Computing

  • Speed Benchmarks: Ari Weinstein noted the long-term goal is for Codex to operate 2x to 10x faster than a human, making it an indispensable tool for routine computing.
  • End-to-End Task Management: The vision is to transition Codex from a coding assistant to a general "teammate" capable of handling administrative burdens, such as updating financial spreadsheets in local apps like Numbers.
  • Platform Availability: Computer use is currently available for Codex users on Mac, with a Windows version confirmed to be in development.
"A lot of computer use implementations... take over your entire computer so you can't use your computer while the agent is using your apps. [With Codex], you can keep on using your computer while Codex is working in the background." — Ari Weinstein
"We pull a bunch of information that is textual describing the interface... it can understand more deeply the role of each element that's on-screen. This just makes the model super accurate at performing tasks." — Ari Weinstein
"I think where we are going to want to get to is a place where computer use is superhuman... where computer use can operate a computer two, five, ten times as fast as a person." — Ari Weinstein

Yeah, we've put together some really exciting things with the way that it works with the model, and it's just only the beginning of the kind of work that we're able to do. Historically, computer use has been only something that works with screenshots. It takes advantage of the power of multimodal models so the model can see the interface and click and type by coordinate which is great. The model can use that to see things even that are scrolled off-screen. It can understand more deeply the role of each element that's on-screen. And so this just makes the model super accurate at performing tasks. And then the other benefit of it which you were alluding to is that because it doesn't require images necessarily, we can use non-multimodal models like Codex Spark which are super fast. And so all of a sudden you have this experience where computer use can, when you use one of those models, use software even faster than you can. That's amazing, so cool. So we see it here like open the text, type the message now to me, and in a second it's sent. That's pretty incredible. Pretty sick. So it just did this in the background. I was able to do other things on my computer at the same time and it's super fast. I just got the ding and we got it. I have it, very nice. Asking me to try computer use for debugging apps. Sick, incredible. And you brought so much from your knowledge of Sky into the Codex app now. This is incredible. Yeah, earlier products like Operator and ChatGPT Agent... for those products we used to train dedicated models for computer use and since then the research team has done this amazing work to actually bring those capabilities into the main GPT models. And so now we're actually building this on Codex on the same models that are available through the API, and everyone can build these amazing computer use capabilities. So that's been super super nice and also great streamlining for our workflow internally. I think that, you know, it's amazing how fast we've been able to get this to work with the mainline models and with Spark. But I think where we are going to want to get to is a place where computer use is superhuman. You know, I think that we can get to a place where computer use can operate a computer two, five, ten times as fast as a person. And I think that's where it's going to become indispensable. You're going to want to use it for so many computing tasks, for really everything you do in your life. It's going to save you so much time and let you focus on the things that are important, and so I'm really excited about what the roadmap looks like there. One thing I wanted to touch on maybe that people might be curious about is like the safety approach to all of this. You have these amazing capabilities for Codex to now kind of drive some apps on your Mac. How are you guys all thinking about like safety? Yeah, it's such a good question. I feel like this type of technology has the potential to be kind of scary because it's actually taking over, you know, the actions that you would do on your computer and it has access to so much stuff. So we feel like it's so important that people feel really comfortable using this technology and so we've been spending a lot of time thinking about how to do that. One of the things that we've done here is we've made computer use such that it can only access applications that you allow. Every time Codex goes to use an app for the first time, it asks for your permission. When you say yes, Codex can see and type into that app, but it can't see or interact with any other app on your computer. So if you've some stuff that's, you know, maybe a little bit sensitive in one of your applications, you can feel very confident knowing that Codex can access your developer applications and your productivity applications without accessing anything that's more sensitive. That just builds a lot of trust, I think, for the user. Absolutely, yeah, that's pretty amazing because it's not like streaming your entire desktop or accessing all of your files or anything like that. It's very much like case by case, app by app. As you're trying to be productive you're giving Codex the permission to do so. I mean obviously this is a simple task but now that we've seen the power of kind of computer use, I'm curious. Like what have you used computer use in Codex for? Yeah, I have all these spreadsheets that I use for like financial tracking and now I actually ask Codex to update them for me and I don't have to do it myself anymore. Super super powerful. Like nowadays when I want to start something new, whether it's programming or even something else on my computer, I feel like I want to turn to Codex first because it saves me so much time.
