How We Grew Koch Industries to $150 Billion Without Going Public: Charles & Chase Koch

All-In Podcast(1.1M)·95:27·3.5K views·2026-05-12
Charles KochChairman of the Board, Koch Industries Chase KochPresident, Koch Disruptive Technologies David FriedbergCo-host, All-In Podcast
TL;DRKoch Industries scaled 9,000x over six decades into a $150 billion private powerhouse by remaining "capability-bounded" rather than industry-bound, reinvesting 90% of its profits, and practicing a decentralized management philosophy focused on…
bottom-up empowerment,...

TL;DR

Koch Industries scaled 9,000x over six decades into a $150 billion private powerhouse by remaining "capability-bounded" rather than industry-bound, reinvesting 90% of its profits, and practicing a decentralized management philosophy focused on bottom-up empowerment, value-first hiring, and constructive learning from failure.

The "Capability-Bounded" Growth Strategy

  • Defining the Philosophy: Rather than restricting growth to a single sector, Koch scales by applying established capabilities—such as logistics, operations, trading, and branding—to entirely new industries.
      • The "Republic of Science" Model: Unlike Berkshire Hathaway’s siloed, hands-off conglomerate structure, Koch operates as an integrated network of capabilities shared across business platforms.
  • Aggressive Reinvestment: Koch Industries routinely reinvests 90% of its profits back into new growth and acquisitions, allowing the company to think in terms of decades rather than quarters.
  • Scale and Scope:
      • Growth Metrics: Since Charles Koch joined full-time in 1961, the company has grown from 300 employees to over 130,000 across 60 countries, increasing its valuation 9,000-fold.
      • Diverse Footprint: Operating eight wholly-owned platforms spanning refining, chemicals, fertilizers, forest and consumer products, building products, electronics (Molex), software, and four distinct investment arms.

Principle-Based Management and Culture

  • Bottom-Up Empowerment: Koch focuses on creating a culture where employees are empowered to act without needing top-down control, maximizing the collective knowledge of the entire workforce.
  • Value-First Hiring: Recruitment prioritizing values and integrity over technical skill, and placing credentials last on the hierarchy of importance.
      • The Farm Team Approach: Actively hiring individuals with strong work ethics and a contribution-motivated mindset.
      • Case Study (Jared Benson): Koch's current Chief Information Officer (CIO) started with the company 20 years ago striping parking lots. He possessed no college degree but rose through the ranks by demonstrating value-creation in cybersecurity and data science.
  • Organizational Social Mimicry: Rather than implementing company-wide mandates (referred to as "sheep-dipping"), Koch installs management principles in small, struggling business units first. When these units succeed, other teams naturally copy their methods.

Leveraging Failure and Cultural Transformation in Acquisitions

  • The Role of Failure: Koch treats failure as a mechanism for "experimental discovery." A good experiment is defined as one where the value of the learning gained exceeds the financial cost of the failure.
      • Historical Failures: The "gas-to-bread spread" vertical integration attempt in the late 1990s and acquiring hundreds of millions in out-of-the-money hog contracts without due diligence taught leadership the dangers of violating the scientific method and failing to properly align talent values.
  • Transformative Acquisitions:
      • Georgia-Pacific (2005): A massive $20 billion bet. Koch turned around GP's highly bureaucratic, top-down culture by dismantling executive privileges (such as private elevator access on the 51st floor) and reorganizing corporate structures to align with front-line operations.
      • Molex (2013): Acquired for its electronic connector capabilities, Koch transformed Molex from a public-company mindset focused purely on short-term quarterly revenue to a long-term bottom-line value approach by reshaping leadership.

Philanthropy, Education Reform, and Social Change

  • The "Stand Together" Movement: Founded in 2003, Stand Together is a community of nearly 1,000 business leaders dedicated to removing institutional barriers that prevent individuals from realizing their potential.
  • Individualized Education: Advocating for a shift away from the traditional "teach-to-test" classroom structure toward highly customized, project-based learning.
      • Key Initiatives: Supporting innovators like Sal Khan (Khan Academy) and Joe Alimandi (Alpha School), who uses gamification to solve the student motivation gap.
      • Vela Fund: A joint venture with the Walton family applying venture capital models to seed over 5,000 micro-schools across the United States.
  • Non-Partisan Policy Engagement: Learning from early political setbacks (including Charles Koch's historical involvement with the Libertarian Party), the organization now follows Frederick Douglass's advice to "work with anyone to do right and no one to do wrong."

Capitalism, AI, and the Future of Economic Mobility

  • Restoring Economic Mobility: Charles Koch argues that both political parties have stalled upward mobility by creating corporate welfare, excessive occupational licensing, and tariffs that disrupt comparative advantages.
  • AI as a Socratic Tool: Koch advocates for "permissionless innovation" to lower the barrier of entry for learning.
      • Internal AI Deployment: Koch developed the *Principle Companion* app (powered by GPT/Claude models) to guide employees through complex business and life problems using Socratic questioning based on the company's core management principles.

Notable Quotes

"If you're not failing at everything, you're not doing anything new." — *Charles Koch*
"Our talent vision is: values first, skill second, credentials last." — *Chase Koch*
"A good experiment is where the value of what you learn from this failure is higher than the cost of the experiment." — *Charles Koch*
"Any good partnership of any kind... requires three things: it requires shared vision, shared values, and having complementary capabilities that you use to make each other better." — *Charles Koch*
What an honor to be here. Thank you for hosting us, Forbes, and welcome. This will be put out as the All-In interview, so I'm really excited to share this conversation with everyone in the world, on the internet. And to get some time with Charles Koch, Chase Koch. Chase and I have known each other since 2013, when we overlapped in the agriculture industry. We got to know each other, and we've been business partners. Charles and I have also gotten to know each other a few times over the years, but I'm really excited for this conversation tonight. So, Charles, thank you for being here. Thanks for having us. It's an honor. I'm going all in. Every few years, a new ad channel opens before the market catches on. That's axon.ai right now, the AI ad platform behind one of the biggest runs in tech, with access to over a billion daily active users. Full-screen video ads in mobile games are watched for a median of 35 seconds. Businesses are profitably spending hundreds of thousands of dollars a day on it, and most advertisers don't even know it exists yet. The window is open at axon.ai/allin. I'm going all in. In Silicon Valley, entrepreneurs and even mature company CEOs always like to learn about the story of other businesses and the success of those businesses. I've always felt like Koch Industries was that untold story of probably the most profitable private, family-owned business in the world. Maybe I'm off on a couple of points, but they are certainly up there. It is one of the most impressive business stories because of the evolution of the business, which I'm hopeful we can hear a little bit about how that evolution came to be tonight. Just for some statistics, if Koch were publicly traded, the revenue would put it easily in the top 25 of the Fortune 500. It's a family-owned business based out of Wichita, founded in 1940 by Fred Koch. Their businesses range from energy, agriculture, chemicals, building products, consumer products, and even cloud computing, to a very active minority investment portfolio with 120,000 plus employees—that statistic might be off—across 60 countries. They have a very unique operating model, which we'll get into today, including principles around disruptive innovation of the business, reinvesting 90% of profits in new businesses and growth, and meritocratic values. I'm hopeful that tonight we can take an opportunity to hear about the evolution of the business and talk about some of those principles. Maybe we can get started, Charles, if you could give us a sense of the scale of the business. What are the business lines that you operate today, and maybe provide a little more color to those high-level statistics I shared? I can go back through some of the history and the failures and successes, but I'll go through what we've grown since the early 1960s. Back then we had 300 employees; now we have more than 130,000 across 60 countries. We have increased in value 9,000 times over that period. When did you join the business? In 1961 full-time. I'd been working... well, my father... we lived on a farm and he told me at age six he didn't want me to be a country club bum. So, he made me work in all my spare time, which I hated, and so I was always in trouble. He was kind of tough on me, rightfully so, and thank God he did. Years later, I asked him, "Pop, why were you so much tougher on me than you were on my younger brothers?" And he said, "Son, you plumb wore me out." When you came into the business, what was the scope of the business? What was the business operating? Okay, we had two main businesses. One was to design and make fractionating trays. That is, they separate liquids by differences in boiling points. And then our largest business was a crude oil gathering system in Oklahoma. And so, my father... and I was... I had finished MIT just a few years earlier. I was working for Arthur D. Little, then a leading consulting firm. You'll think this is a joke, but at age 25, I was doing management consulting. I mean, I have to laugh at the absurdity of that, but I did. They were paying me for it, believe it or not. So, my father called me and he said, "Son, I want you to come back and join the business." And as tough as he had been on me—and as I say, rightly so—I declined. He called me a few weeks later and he said, "Son, either you come back to run the company, or I'm going to have to sell it because my health is bad, the companies aren't doing well, and I don't have long to live." So, I agreed because of a number of reasons. One, the first one is I got three degrees at MIT in engineering, and I sucked as an engineer. I mean, get that... and people ask, "Well, so how did you get through MIT?" I was really good at the math, the science, and the theory, but I was no good at making or operating things. So, I figured out pretty quickly that I wasn't going to make it as an engineer, and that I needed to be an entrepreneur. Because I was good at principles—and that's why we led—I was always looking for principles that would help me contribute and succeed. That is what transformed our company. So, you came into the business with a couple hundred employees, you said? 300. 300 employees. And how did you think about it? Was the mandate to grow the business, or was it just to keep it stable? No, no, no. Could I take a few minutes and go through those first two businesses, or first three? Okay. You're the show. I'm honored, sounds great. Yeah. Okay. The first one was making fractionating trays, designing those. We had a president then who was... well, one of our principles is you don't want to be top-down and obsessed with controlling everybody. He would send out memos every week demanding details on what they spent, how they spent it, what they did, and why they did this or that. As a matter of fact, they started ignoring him. And then the whole culture was protectionist. That is, when they sold the internals for a fractionating tower, they wouldn't tell the customers the design. The customers would say, "Well, we need to know the design so we can correct it." No, they wouldn't give it to them. And then, what's even worse, to satisfy the European market, they didn't even build a plant there. They had multiple subcontractors do parts of a tray, and then brought them all together and assembled them with another contractor. You can imagine how that was for speed and cost. So, we were losing our ass, if you can excuse the expression. So, I changed the management and changed the philosophy. I said, "Okay, the first thing we're going to focus on is creating value for our customers. The second thing is we're going to empower our employees so they want to do this. And the third thing is we're going to build a plant in Italy to satisfy the European market. We're going to do it all ourselves." And so, we became profitable. Then we started adding related products, which I'll get to later, and we started growing. Can I ask a question? Yeah. You came in at 25, plus or minus a little bit, and you saw the problems in the business. It was not profitable, it was not being well-managed, and you overturned the management team. How did you have the confidence at this age, with the experience you had, to take that level of action so quickly? Well, it was life or death. My father said, "You can run this business any way you want. The only thing you need my approval on is to sell." That's the way he talked me into coming back after I said I didn't want to, or wasn't going to. Then in 1970, what really helped is my younger brother David joined the business, and he continued that growth. And then, you're now running a profitable operation, you've got a European business, and at that point did you start to think about expanding into other products? Well, yes. I was learning all these different principles, and what I saw we were doing, not just here but in other things, was building capabilities. I looked at it and thought, "We need to be capability-bounded, not industry-bounded." For example, you could say to a certain extent, because we were in crude oil gathering, we were in the oil industry. Everybody was saying, "Oh, that means you need to be an integrated oil company; you need to be in everything." They did that blindly, without considering comparative advantage. No, you need to be in the part of the industry—the part of the value chain—where you can create more value than others. Otherwise, you're going to fail. That's what we're seeing happening now; there's more specialization by comparative advantage. So, I created this principle called "creating virtuous cycles of mutual benefit." What that led us to do is to start this never-ending cycle of growth, innovation, success, and failures. When we handled failures right, we learned from them, they made us better, and they taught us how to apply principles to create value. We're still going through that. We have a lot of failures, and when you apply creative destruction to your new projects, if you're not failing sometimes, you're not doing anything new. Where did you learn that lesson? What was the first major failure? You know, they always say you have a plan until you get punched in the face. What was the first punch in the face? Well, I had a bunch of them with that company, which was called Koch Engineering then. Like I said, when we got into refining, we created petroleum coke, so I said, "Let's come up with a way to use that as a base to make activated carbon." That was a failure, and we spent a fair amount of money on it. We had a whole bunch of those, and we've had many more since. How did you make the decision to shut it down or walk away? At some point, a lot of entrepreneurs have this problem: they build something, they're too in love with it, and they don't know when to say enough is enough. Yeah, well, enough is enough when we lose our ass enough. No, really, it's when we decide we don't have the capability to create superior value for our customers and be rewarded for it. Sometimes it can be the structure of a business. For example, with InSightec, a company funded by the group Chase founded, Koch Disruptive Technologies, it does tremendous things, but it has a structure that makes it hard to make it profitable. That is the other thing. These are principles we've learned. We ask, "Okay, what were the principles we didn't apply that caused us to fail?" That's what I mean when I say we learn from failure. You asked about the businesses we're in now. Chase, and the other Koch people here, you all can catch me up on the ones I miss. We have engineered products, engineering construction, we build solar plants, we have commodity trading and distribution, we have fertilizers, and we have refined products. We also have chemicals and polymers, glass, forest and consumer products, four different investment firms with different comparative advantages, electrical products, and software systems for management. Dave, let me just hit one point. No, you got it, you did a great job. I mean, there are basically eight wholly-owned business unit platforms that he described, and then four different investment businesses. I wanted to really drill a point home, because when I started hanging out with you and the tech community to build that network, a lot of people had the same question you did: "Who is Koch? What are you guys all about?" I know it's a large private business, but being based in Wichita, Kansas, people don't know that much about it. I think the point that makes Koch so different from almost any other company out there is what my father said about being capability-bounded, not industry-bounded. How do you get from a small crude oil gathering company in southern Oklahoma to all of those businesses he described? While the principles are present throughout, which we'll talk about in this discussion, one of the absolute core differences is that whole approach to capabilities. I'd encourage anyone in business who is trying to scale to think about it through that lens: what capabilities have I demonstrated that can add value to customers? Then, point those capabilities at new industries where you can experiment. This is one of our core principles—experimental discovery. Instead of trying to do everything at once and conquering the world, experiment and test to see whether the customer actually values your product. Along the way, our core capabilities started off as operations, logistics, and trading. In the very early days of Koch, that's what we demonstrated we were good at, and we were getting great customer feedback. Because we had this capability approach, we said, "Okay, we started in energy with crude oil gathering, pipelines, and refineries. Can we point those same capabilities into natural gas? Can we point them into chemicals, so let's experiment there? Can we point them into fertilizers, because then we learn about natural gas?" Then the Georgia-Pacific opportunity came along, and it was like, "Hey, these are wood products. It doesn't seem similar to these other businesses, but it uses the same core capabilities." We bought Georgia-Pacific, and along the way, it was somewhat of a happy accident that we started learning about consumer products and branding. Branding became a new capability for Koch through acquisition, but it started with asking, "Where do we think we can add value?" and collecting new capabilities along the way. I think that's a very simple way to think about Koch over the course of time and how we're different. One other thing I'll mention, because I've been asked many times, "Is it sort of like a Berkshire Hathaway, where you have all these different businesses in a conglomerate?" I would say no. Obviously, Warren Buffett and his team have done an unbelievable job operating their business the way they do, but we think about our business very differently. Instead of operating them all as independent businesses in silos, we think about it as a Republic of Science. We're not a conglomerate; we're an integrated set of capabilities. Is it fair to say that you wouldn't consider an acquisition or a new business line if there wasn't some relatedness to an existing competency at the company? It depends. As you'll see when you read the book, we have a chapter on creative destruction and what Schumpeter called all the different ways to do that. One way is to create a new management approach, and that's our biggest one. For example, when we bought Molex, which makes electrical connectors, it is a fantastic business, but at first, it wasn't doing great. We said, "We think if we can get them to apply these principles, it will turn them around." The problem when we do that is the tendency to learn the lingo; people call everything by these new names, but they still do what they always did. That's what was going on there. So, finally, we said, "No, we have to change the management." Once we did that and they started applying these principles, they took off, and now they're knocking it out of the park. But let me go back to failures. I mean, we're understating our great strength in failures. I'll give you our worst failures and what caused them: they were caused by violating the principle of hiring people first on values, and second on talent. For years, I've told our people, "Look, if you want to hire somebody with bad values because you like them, hire them slow and stupid so we can catch them real quick and get them the hell out." Maybe get them to go to work for our competitors or something. But anyway, that was huge. Then we made that even worse by taking people who had terrible values and making them leaders. What we want instead is for everyone in the company to be contribution-motivated. That is: "I want to succeed by contributing. I want to be rewarded for my contributions, not for anything else." It is about the value I create for our customers or for the future. Unfortunately, some of these people were destructively motivated. What they wanted was power or control, and they would hide their failures and make up their successes. I'll give you two examples. One goes back to 1973—you remember the war in the Middle East and everything—and they had gotten us into all kinds of wild, reckless trades that could have bankrupted the company. And then later—to show you that repetition penetrates even the dullest of minds—I needed this to happen a bunch of times before I finally thought, "Okay, I got it. God, please don't punish me anymore for my stupid mistakes." Around the same time, in our agricultural group, we put in leaders who were destructively motivated, and in refining, we got in a leader, and they were destroying those businesses. It didn't quite bankrupt us, but it almost wiped out all of Koch's earnings in the late 1990s. So, does that give you a flavor? Yeah, very much so. You'll appreciate this, being an agriculture guy. To go a little deeper on what happened in the late '90s in our ag business, we called it the "gas-to-bread spread" strategy. We wanted to basically be in every element of the value chain, all the way from pulling the natural gas out of the ground, converting it into fertilizer, and making the nitrogen products to grow the crops that would ultimately end up on the grocery store shelves. In pursuit of bread, we got into pizza crusts and all this crazy stuff. When you look back on it, you're like, "What the hell were we doing, right?" But it was exactly what he was saying about leadership thinking they can do anything. The idea that if you control the entire value chain, you can make it successful completely violates probably all 41 principles in the book, right? Principles like experimental discovery, knowing where your capabilities are, and putting the right people in the right roles. So, yes, we called it the gas-to-bread spread. Some people called it the "ass-to-bread spread" too. And there's another principle in there: integrity. Because when they knew there were losses in some of these units, they wouldn't tell us; they wanted to go ahead anyway. We had a deal within that, like the Purina large animal feed business. One of the things acquired was the large animal feed business, Purina... It was mainly hogs. Yeah, hog feed. We did no due diligence, which violates one of our principles: apply the scientific method to disprove your hypothesis as much as you try to prove it. We closed that acquisition, and within days, we found out that we had hundreds of millions of dollars in out-of-the-money hog contracts because we didn't even look at the contracts. I think this is really important for founders who want to grow. You get into this grow-at-all-costs mindset, you stop asking "why not," and this is the kind of trouble you get yourself into. Let's go back to the management piece. How do you take these principles, which you've applied successfully to... I would use the term "iterate," because for me, failure is all about iterating to success, finding paths and businesses that work, and ultimately finding people that work. How do you drive a culture that represents these principles? You could write a book, give it to all your employees, and say, "Guys, here are 41 principles. We've sat down, thought about them, and written them down; they're going to work." But to actually live them, realize them, and hold people not just responsible but accountable to them—how did you do that as you developed this over the decades? At first, we tried to get them to do it through "sheep-dipping." That is, you take everybody in, give them a big seminar, and say, "Now go do this." If you want to read a book that's hard to read—I mean, if you want a really hard one, you can read *Human Action*, but this is even harder—it's called *Personal Knowledge* by Michael Polanyi, who was a chemist and then became a philosopher. He goes through what it takes to develop personal knowledge. You have to rewire your brain to have it work differently. You have a habit so you don't need to think about it, but then if you want to change—like, "Do I brush my teeth first or do I comb my hair first? No, I want to start combing my hair first"—all of a sudden, you're back brushing your teeth first because you're not thinking about it. Your brain gets wired that way. Let's say you're a weightlifter and you want to be a marathoner. It's going to take intense work over time to change your body. Well, your brain is part of your body, so you've got to do the same thing. So we said, "Okay, let's find a group that's really interested in this, that is struggling or having problems. Here are the principles, and we'll coach them and help them start doing it." If they work with intensity on it and succeed, we don't need sheep-dipping anymore. The other businesses and capability groups will say, "Gosh, I'd like to do that." You don't need to call them in; instead, we have more demand for people who can help them. The hardest thing is having people in strategy or our principle-based management group who are really good at helping them, because they are in more demand than anybody. The best thing is that success will drive social mimicry; you'll see others follow. Here's another take on what he's saying that really connects culture to your question. The essence of principle-based management and all the principles in this book is: what if you could have a business and a culture—small, medium, or large—where everyone knew what to do without being told? That is hard to get your head around, right? Most businesses come at it from the top down, where there is an iconic leader who is the smartest guy in the room, building the strategy and telling everyone what to do. I think one of the most important principles in this book, which we tell a lot of stories around, is to flip that on its head. It's about bottom-up empowerment with principles, empowering your talent, your team, and your leaders with these principles so that you use the collective knowledge of everyone, not just a couple of smart guys at the top of the company. Most people in most enterprises who aren't owner-operators don't want to fail; they want to keep their jobs and move up the ladder by being repeatedly successful. If you want to create a culture of creative destruction, a culture of failing and learning from failure, it's very hard to get salaried individuals to do that. If they fail once, and then they fail again, they start thinking, "I'm worried about losing my job." What you typically see in most scaled organizations when founders or owners don't operate anymore is middle and senior management saying, "I'm going to take the less risky path. I'm going to do the less creatively disruptive thing. I'm going to do the thing that's least likely to fail because I don't want to lose my job. I want to keep my job, get my bonus, move on to year two, and go home to my family." That becomes their objective. That approach creates perverse incentives. We try to align our incentives to reward people according to their overall contribution to Koch's future. For example, if they run an experiment—and that doesn't mean doing what we did in the agricultural group where you buy all these hogs and lose hundreds of millions of dollars, which is not a real experiment—a good experiment is one where what you learn from the failure is of higher value than the cost of the experiment itself. When we do that, we are evaluating how the person is building capability for the future. We put so much emphasis on asking, "Are you building capability?" and part of that capability is the culture. What Chase did with Koch Labs when he started Koch Disruptive Technologies was say, "I want every business to be a laboratory for what we find, both to help us source these tech opportunities, and to try things out in that business if they want to experiment." Being in all these different businesses that touch almost every part of the economy gives us a big advantage. It has affected the whole culture. In your business, don't you want to be part of Koch Labs? People feel like, "We're an experimental discovery group, we're not just a bunch of grunts here grinding stuff out." I think the KDT example is a really good one because you asked about motivation and what happens if you fail and get fired. We tried to take a little bit of the Silicon Valley approach and bring in experimental discovery. You learn more, and you can pivot. You might have a failure, but you say, "Now I know what I don't want to do, so I'm going to pivot my strategy to what we want to keep trying," as long as you don't sink the company with some massive bet, right? I think KDT was a great experience. When we made those first investments—you know this in venture—the losers fall out first, and the winners take a hell of a lot longer to materialize. If we had judged it strictly based on saying, "Okay guys, you've got three or four years to figure this out," we would have shut down KDT. But it was that experimental discovery principle and mindset that we applied to it. By the way, we were learning so much as a company just from seeing the technologies coming around the corner that might disrupt our core business. We valued that learning, and we rewarded the people who were bringing that knowledge in. If you just looked at it on a bottom-line basis in the first couple of years, you would have said, "Just shut this down." Right? Over time, those investments matured and the returns started to come because we thought long-term about it. It all came from that experimental discovery principle, along with creative destruction. We realized, "If we're not in the game on technology and we don't see what's coming, something is going to happen." Especially with how fast technology is moving today, some of our businesses are going to become dinosaurs. How much of that risk were you willing to take, and did you take it on acquisitions? Doing homegrown experiments on new business ideas, strategies, and products can be lower cost, but if you're going to do an acquisition, do you have less room for error? Would you say that that business unit operates like the rest of Koch Industries today? Oh, absolutely. I have to say, that is such a rare and difficult thing to pull off. There are countless stories of acquisitions where the acquirer thinks they have the culture and knows how to transfer it, yet literally no one seems to be able to do it. This was one of the insights from Warren Buffett: you find great managers, let them continue to operate as owners of that business with a profit share, and because they have a durable moat, you make a long-term investment and just leave them alone. Yeah, but see, that wouldn't work for us with the things we buy. Let me give you another example that was even more difficult. My father died not too long after I joined the company in 1967, and we owned an interest in a small refinery in Minnesota. Two years later, we were able to buy it. It was not being operated very well because management had let the union control how it was run, so it was run very inefficiently. The first thing we tried to do was change the work rules. They went on strike and, by the way, that was right at the start of my honeymoon. Thanks a lot, guys. It was violent; they ran a switch engine and tried to knock down one of our units, shot high-power rifles into the plant, and blocked the gates. It was impossible to get in there, so we had to use helicopters. But we were successful in operating without the union workers for nine months by bringing people in from other plants, and it actually operated better than it had before. Finally, we got the work rules changed, and then we said, "Okay, we're going to empower the employees and change the culture." If you think Georgia-Pacific was tough, this was much tougher. We worked and worked on it to make their jobs better, get their opinions, get them to work as teams, and encourage them to come up with innovations. When they did, we would reward them, and we got the union to agree with that. For instance, one group of them said, "We're buying all these spare parts we need, but if you build us a machine shop, we can make them cheaper and faster ourselves." They did exactly that, and they saved us a ton of money and made things more efficient. Now, the culture there is fantastic. Keep in mind, we're not talking about Wichita; we're talking about Minnesota. We are so proud of what they've done. It still blows me away how much they've taken these principles to heart to make that place successful. We've increased the capacity tenfold, and it is now one of the best refineries in the country. Pop, wouldn't you say the common theme on all of these is the same story we had with Molex? It was similar to Georgia-Pacific, but different. In almost every case—Pop, tell me if you agree with this—it requires changing the leadership to people who have a paradigm of bottom-up empowerment, and who will learn and apply the principles. Almost every time we fail, it comes down to ignoring the principles. You can reverse engineer these stories, which is what the book is about, and see, "Well, we missed that principle, or we missed this principle." Ultimately, it comes down to talent and having the right people with the right mindset. I want to share just one story that shows how we apply this at Koch. My father talked about our talent vision being culture first and skill second, or values first and skill second. I always add a third dimension to that as well: values first, skill second, and credentials last. That is a very different mindset than most companies have. Most companies look at candidates and say, "I want the guys who have a 4.0 from an Ivy League school." Obviously, there are some incredibly smart folks from those schools. But in our experience—and this is also one of the reasons we stayed in Wichita, Kansas—we can basically hire the "farm team." These are kids who grew up on the farm, who have a contribution-motivated mindset, who work their tails off, and who want to come in and make a difference, as opposed to coming in expecting things with an entitlement mindset. Case in point, our CIO today is named Jared Benson. His first interaction with Koch was striping lines in our parking lot. He came in and proved himself, running circles around a lot of the team with his contribution-motivated mindset. He saw the cybersecurity risk and that wave coming, built a whole capability to protect us from cyberattacks, and now he is the CIO of the company—a guy with no college degree. That kind of mindset defines our talent vision: values first, and hiring people who just want to come in, love the job, and make a difference. Ajay Banga - President of the World Bank Group | Investment Conference 2026

Norges Bank Investment Management(110.0K)·11:37·1.3K views·2026-05-12
Ajay BangaPresident, World Bank Group
TL;DROrganizational culture is a force multiplier defined by "what happens when the leader is not in the room," requiring a high Decency Quotient (DQ), radical simplicity in mission, and transparent, focused measurement to drive global transformation.
Culture is often dismissed...

TL;DR

Organizational culture is a force multiplier defined by "what happens when the leader is not in the room," requiring a high Decency Quotient (DQ), radical simplicity in mission, and transparent, focused measurement to drive global transformation.

Redefining Culture as the "Organizational Glue"

Culture is often dismissed as abstract, but it serves as the fundamental driver of how an organization makes decisions, takes risks, and delivers on its mission.

  • Behavioral Measurement: Culture is not a value statement or a poster; it is defined by what is measured, what is rewarded, and—most importantly—what is tolerated.
  • The Force Multiplier: True culture exists when employees make the right decisions autonomously. It shifts the organizational default from "No" to "Yes, if..."
  • Leadership Responsibility: Leadership is an earned responsibility to create conditions where teams feel supported rather than policed.

The Decency Quotient (DQ)

While IQ and EQ are standard metrics for leadership, Banga introduces "DQ" as the essential component for building trust and speed.

  • Fairness Over Niceness: DQ is not about being "nice." It is about being fair, direct, and consistent. It requires telling the truth even when uncomfortable and providing feedback with absolute clarity.
  • Driving Speed: A high DQ increases organizational velocity because employees trust the system. When people feel safe to challenge ideas, decision-making improves.
  • Hand vs. Finger: Effective leadership should feel like a "hand on the back" (pushing people forward) rather than a "finger in the face" (intimidation). Fear may drive short-term results but fails to build judgment or initiative.

Clarity of Purpose Through Radical Simplicity

In large organizations like Mastercard or the World Bank, the primary obstacle to progress is often confusion rather than active resistance.

  • The Hardship of Simplicity: Making a complex mission simple is a leader's most difficult task. Speaking without jargon ensures that the mission is accessible to every level of the workforce.
  • "Kill Cash" (Mastercard): To pivot from a payments processor to a technology platform, Banga unified the company under the simple goal of killing cash. This clarity encompassed everything from data analytics to financial inclusion.
  • "Job Creation" (World Bank): To combat poverty, Banga simplified the World Bank’s multifaceted efforts into a single focus: creating jobs.
      • State of Being vs. Mind: Poverty is a state of "being" (solved by aid) and a state of "mind" (solved by the dignity and optimism of productive work).
  • Relentless Repetition: Leaders must never tire of repeating the core mission. Organizations require constant reinforcement until the message dictates daily decision-making.

Accountability and Measurement

Aligning incentives is critical to cultural shifts, particularly when moving an organization from vague goals to concrete outcomes.

  • The Power of Sunlight: Banga advocates for radical transparency, stating that "sunlight is the best disinfectant."
  • Scorecard Reduction: Upon joining the World Bank, Banga reduced the corporate scorecard from 155 items to 22.
      • Focus: A 155-item list allows a leader to hide failures behind a sea of "A+" grades on minor tasks.
      • Transparency: All 22 metrics are now tracked publicly on the World Bank website, allowing shareholders to monitor progress in real-time.
  • Public vs. Private Levers: While private sector leaders can use compensation and capital allocation to drive change, public sector leaders must rely on aligning teams around outcomes and simplifying structures to overcome risk aversion and bureaucracy.
"Culture is what gets measured, what gets rewarded, what gets tolerated. But most importantly, it's what happens when you're not in the room." — Ajay Banga
"Decency is not about being nice. It's actually about being fair, direct, and consistent... It allows you to move faster because people trust the system." — Ajay Banga
"Leadership has to feel like a hand on your back—you’ve gotta push people forward—but not a finger in their face." — Ajay Banga
"Simplicity is the key... Conversing in simple terms without jargon is the world's most difficult thing to do as a leader." — Ajay Banga
Our first speaker is Ajay Banga, the president of the World Bank Group. He's led cultural transformation twice: once in a phenomenal manner at Mastercard, where he was the CEO for 12 years, and now at the World Bank. The private sector tends to move faster. The public sector has decades of history, multiple shareholders, and stakeholders. How do you drive cultural transformation when the rules are completely different? We'd love to hear more. Please welcome Ajay. So they say that culture trumps strategy every time, and the pun is intended. So that's where we're going to start from. Everyone in this room has built or led organizations. You've all done something of that type. You've shaped teams, and you've made decisions under pressure. You have lived the reality that culture is not something abstract. You've got your own hard-won views on it, no doubt. But I'm not here to tell you that I've figured it out. I just have an unusual vantage point, as you said. I've had the chance to be the CEO of a public company, that's Mastercard, and now at the World Bank Group. It's quite different. It's 25,000 plus people with 189 governments as my shareholders, with 25 executive directors resident in my building with 20 advisors each. This means a lot of people who want to know what I'm doing. So, I can't really goof off either. What we all know is that as a CEO, culture is what determines our success. I think culture is the single most important thing all of us work on. It's easy to talk about it, but what exactly is the darn thing? I think what is it and how do you build it are the two most important questions that most of us have to answer. So, in an effort to be faithful to Nicolai, I actually have written a speech down on culture, which is pretty interesting. I've never done that, but I'm going to read this out to you so I don't miss a few things. I spent a lot of time working on this one. I think what culture is depends on your organization. Regardless of your unique circumstances, culture is the glue. It is absolutely the glue. It is how people make decisions, how they take a risk, and how they work together to deliver whatever your mission is. It's how you drive an organization to say "yes, if," rather than "no." Every organization has plenty of people incented to say no. There are never enough people who say, "yes, if you do it this way." Finding that culture is important. Culture is not a value statement. It is not a poster on the wall, and definitely it is not what the CEO says at town halls. It's just not. At the simplest, culture is what gets measured, what gets rewarded, and what gets tolerated. But most importantly, it's what happens when you're not in the room. And so that's the culture that I'm talking about. I think the idea of "it's what happens when you're not in the room" is what makes it a force multiplier. The how is where culture lives or dies. While everybody's context is different, there are some common things between the public and the private sector, and there are some very big differences. First is what I call the decency quotient, DQ. I learned very quickly that IQ and EQ were important while I was growing up, but I believe that now what you need in leadership is what's called the decency quotient. And I just named it that. I was making a joke in a town hall and I'd just come out of listening to Warren Buffett talk about DQ—in his case, that was Dairy Queen, the ice cream. You can see how my mind works, and I was looking for something to tell people what I meant and to give a word to what I was trying to explain about culture. That was how this idea of decency quotient came. But decency is not about being nice. It's actually about being fair, direct, and consistent. Tell the truth to your people, even when it's uncomfortable. Give that feedback with clarity. Speak up when it's difficult. Treat people with respect. All that doesn't actually slow the organization down; it does quite the opposite because it allows you to move faster because people trust the system. It improves decisions because people are willing to challenge and to be challenged. It actually works in the right way if you do it the right way. Fear drives behavior for a while, but it doesn't build judgment, it doesn't build ownership, and it doesn't build initiative. Decency does. Leadership has to feel like a hand on your back. You gotta push people forward, but not a finger in their face. And that's what decency quotient is all about. That's the first thing. The second one is clarity of purpose. In a large organization, I think the biggest cultural problem is often not resistance—and James can talk to this ad nauseam—it's actually confusion more than resistance. Clarity in his case, in Morgan Stanley, he very clearly delivered a message of what they were going to focus on, which was wealth management as the way that Morgan Stanley would grow. That changed an organization that had multiple ideas of how they would grow to become very focused and very simple. I think simplicity is the key. Making something simple is actually the hardest thing to do. People think that in simplicity you've lost detail. Actually, conversing in simple terms without jargon is the world's most difficult thing to do as a leader. At Mastercard, the company had to redefine itself from a payments processor to a technology platform. How did I simplify it? I united everybody around one set of mission, and it said "kill cash." Just kill cash. That was compared to thinking about everything else. That included technology, it included payments, it included analytics, it included data, it included anything that you could do. Financial inclusion, working with the downtrodden, and getting social security payments into their hands were part of it. Figuring out digital identities all came inside the idea of killing cash. It gave them a very clear sense of direction. At the World Bank, it is a similar issue. How do you kill poverty? I've come around to saying instead of having many different causes—let's do this for climate, let's do that for gender, let's build the hospital, let's do a skilling center, let's do schools—at the end of the day, what you're trying to do to kill poverty is to give people a job. This isn't necessarily working for a big company or a small company or as an entrepreneur. It could be just staying in their small farm and being content with the productivity they get by connecting up with technology to a cooperative. I'm just giving you many ways to do this. The point is, a job earning is the best way to drive people's hope and dignity. If you sort of think about this carefully, a friend of mine once said that poverty is both a state of mind and a state of being. "Being" you can solve by giving grants, by giving aid, by giving donations, or by writing philanthropic dollars. "Mind" doesn't get solved with that. Mind needs you to feel productive every morning, optimistic, and feeling that you've got a future. I think the most important thing is a job gives you both a way to defeat a state of being and a state of mind to drive a nail into the coffin of poverty. That's the clarity that has settled in. Once you start doing that, you get this multiplier effect of people making decisions on it. The last thing about clarity is to never tire of discussing what you've decided is very simple. Leaders get bored saying the same things, but organizations don't get bored hearing it. If you want something to stick, you've got to say it again and again and again and again until it shows up in how people make their decisions. So that was the second topic. The third is to measure what matters and align your incentives. You guys are all pretty clear on that. At Mastercard, we had some simple metrics. At the World Bank, I had a corporate scorecard. For those of you from the private sector, you will love this: I had a corporate scorecard of 155 items when I showed up. I tried to explain to my board of those 25 people that 155 items is about 130 too many for a corporate scorecard. At 155 items, I will always get an A+ because I would have delivered on 30 and you'll never get me to discuss the other 125. That's how it works. It took a long time, but we got ourselves down to 22 items. All 22 are now on our website. You can go and click on them and you can track where I am every month, project by project, on delivering against that. I keep telling our board, sunlight is the best disinfectant. Transparency of your targets and simplifying them are what we're trying to do. Now, all this is lovely, but here's the context difference between the private sector and the public sector. In the private sector, pressure comes from markets, shareholders, profit motive, performance, competition, and crisis. You've got clear levers: compensation, hiring, firing, and capital allocation. At Mastercard, if something wasn't working, I could use all those levers, jiggle with them, and fix it quickly. In the public sector, the challenge is completely different. We've got a mission, but we also have 189 shareholders. Each has a different and nuanced way of looking based on their domestic politics, which they bring to my office every day. You've got longstanding processes, tenure is long, and risk aversion is very real. We're kind of like an academic institution trying to get real work done. You can imagine how complex that is. You cannot reset that system overnight. Compensation is not a lever of the type that you have in the private sector. So you work all the levers you have. You align teams around the outcomes, you simplify what you say, you simplify structures, and you become explicit in what gets recognized and what gets funded. It takes longer, but the principle is exactly the same. If you don't align the system, culture will not move. Alignment comes from simplicity and focus and leading from the front with how you speak about what you're doing. So it's just a closing thought for you. I have told everybody who worked with me over the years that leadership is not a privilege; it's not your birthright. You weren't born with it. It is absolutely what you have to earn. At some point in your career, someone trusted you, like someone trusted me with the responsibility for other people. At that point, your job changes forever. It's no longer about just delivering results. It's about creating the conditions where the people who work for you can feel your hand on their back, pushing them forward. That is what culture is all about because, actually, you're not going to be there to put your hand on their back all the time, but they have to feel it anyway. And that's culture. Thank you very much.

Secretary Hegseth, Chairman Caine & PDO OUSW-CFO Hurst Testify Before the HAC FY 27 Budget Request

Department of War(332.0K)·118:16·8.1K views·2026-05-12
Pete HegsethSecretary, Department of War Dan CaineChairman, Department of War Jules Hurst IIIPDO War Department Comptroller
TL;DRThe Department of War's $1.5 trillion FY27 budget request faces intense congressional scrutiny over a controversial "divest-to-invest" aviation procurement strategy, the rising $29 billion cost of the war with Iran, and a new capital expenditure paradigm aimed at rapidly expanding the U.S.

TL;DR

The Department of War's $1.5 trillion FY27 budget request faces intense congressional scrutiny over a controversial "divest-to-invest" aviation procurement strategy, the rising $29 billion cost of the war with Iran, and a new capital expenditure paradigm aimed at rapidly expanding the U.S. defense industrial base to counter China.

Aviation Procurement and the Shift Away from "Divest-to-Invest"

  • Re-evaluating Aviation Sector Cuts: The department's FY27 budget request proposes cutting over $5 billion from the aviation sector, effectively halting current procurement for core Army platforms (including Black Hawk, Chinook, and Apache helicopters).
      • Representative DeLauro and Judge Carter raised concerns that halting these production lines creates a critical near-term capability gap and risks permanently degrading the specialized supplier base and skilled workforce.
      • Secretary Hegseth confirmed the department is reviewing these cuts and will provide the committee with minimum sustaining production rates, supplier-base risks, and mitigation plans for the Black Hawk and Chinook platforms.
  • Moving Beyond Austerity Mindsets: Secretary Hegseth announced the department is actively shedding the "divest-to-invest" austerity strategy—where current platforms are retired early to fund future, unfielded capabilities.
      • The department is actively funding platforms currently utilized on the battlefield (e.g., MQ-9s, A-10s) and has submitted a budget amendment to OMB to secure the future of the E-7 platform.
  • Managing Transition Risks: Chairman Dan Caine emphasized that the Joint Staff is closely tracking transition risks to avoid a mobility, lift, and sustainment gap as legacy platforms are phased out and Future Vertical Lift schedules evolve.

Escalating Iran War Costs and Stockpile Debates

  • Updated War Projections: PDO War Department Comptroller Jules Hurst III testified that the total cost of the war with Iran has risen to approximately $29 billion (up from a $25 billion estimate on April 29, 2026).
      • Expended Materiel: Replacement and repair of expended military hardware (excluding military construction) is currently estimated at $24 billion, which includes the loss of 39 aircraft.
      • Unestimated Costs: Operational fuel replenishment is currently absorbed into O&M costs, but future Military Construction (MilCon) costs for damaged Middle East infrastructure remain unquantified due to geopolitical and posture uncertainties.
  • Munitions & Stockpile Readiness: Despite congressional concerns over depleted stockpiles, U.S. leadership defended current readiness levels.
      • Chairman Caine affirmed that the combatant commands maintain sufficient munitions to execute current tasks.
      • Secretary Hegseth rejected public assertions that U.S. munitions are depleted, stating the $1.5 trillion budget request is explicitly designed to supercharge future munitions manufacturing.
  • Ceasefire Status and "Project Freedom": A bilateral ceasefire between the U.S. and Iran is currently in effect. "Project Freedom"—a CENTCOM-led initiative to coordinate and guide commercial ship traffic through the Strait of Hormuz—was temporarily paused at the request of Pakistan but remains an active operational option.

Pacific Deterrence and Next-Generation Capabilities

  • Countering China's Production Speed: The FY27 budget dedicates $11.7 billion to the Pacific Deterrence Initiative (PDI) to address China as the pacing military and industrial challenge.
      • Chairman Caine emphasized that the U.S. must scale up manufacturing speed across second-, third-, and fourth-tier suppliers to match China’s shipbuilding and production capabilities.
  • Naval Power and Power Projection: Following a record-setting 11-month deployment of the USS *Gerald R. Ford*, officials highlighted the critical role of aircraft carriers as mobile airfields in the Indo-Pacific. Next-generation capabilities like the F-47 and the F-A-XX (slated for a down-select this summer) are deemed vital to operating outside the range of Chinese hypersonic weapons.
  • Nuclear Deterrence and Microreactors: The department is investing $2 billion in FY26 for the Sea-Launched Cruise Missile-Nuclear (SLCM-N) to provide non-strategic options lower on the escalation ladder. Additionally, the department is accelerating nuclear propulsion and the adoption of advanced microreactors (such as the Army's JANUS program) to guarantee resilient expeditionary power in contested environments.

Fiscal Management, Procurement Reform, and Reprogramming

  • Controversy Over Mandatory Funding: Several committee members, including Ms. McCollum and Mr. Morelle, strongly criticized the department's reliance on mandatory funding and reconciliation bills (which account for 38% of the $413 billion FY27 procurement request), arguing it bypasses standard discretionary oversight.
  • The Private Capitalization Paradigm: Secretary Hegseth defended a major procurement shift: private defense primes are now paying for their own plant construction and equipment capitalization (saving taxpayers an estimated $50 billion in upfront infrastructure costs) in exchange for guaranteed 5-to-7-year munitions procurement contracts.
  • Critical Reprogramming Deficits: Ms. McCollum raised alarms over massive unbudgeted operational deficits requiring immediate funding reallocations:
      • National Guard: $600 million in unbudgeted mission requirements.
      • Army Personnel: Nearly $1 billion in personnel shortfalls.
      • Navy Personnel: $907 million in personnel shortfalls.

Regional Security, Border Operations, and Personnel Care

  • Countering Cartel Drone Threats: Cartels are increasingly adopting advanced military tactics, such as "fiber-optic drone systems" learned from the Russia-Ukraine conflict. Joint Interagency Task Force (JIATF) 401, led by Brigadier General Matt Ross, is utilizing U.S. Southwest border test sites for rapid counter-UAS field testing.
  • National Guard Reimbursement Gaps: Mr. Womack highlighted that between FY20 and FY24, states paid over $71 million to the National Guard for community disaster response under Title 32. These funds were never reimbursed to the National Guard Bureau because non-federal reimbursements currently default directly to the U.S. Treasury.
  • JBSA Barracks Reform: In response to reports of unacceptable, unlivable housing conditions for 68W combat medic students at Joint Base San Antonio, Secretary Hegseth promised immediate physical inspections and broader MilCon bureaucratic reforms.
  • Toxic Exposure Records for Veterans: Representative Susie Lee urged the department to immediately release service records for toxic-exposed veterans from the Nevada Test and Training Range (NTTR) to facilitate VA medical coverage. Secretary Hegseth committed to personally reviewing the department's position on these records.

Notable Quotes

"When you mobilize the Guard, you mobilize the United States people... they are now much more than a tactical reserve, they're a strategic reserve." — Chairman Dan Caine
"The primes are now paying for their own plants and equipment. That’s a brand new paradigm. The taxpayer’s not paying for that. That’s $50 billion of value that you don’t have to appropriate because the companies are paying for it themselves." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
"What we cannot afford to do is to create a near-term aviation gap, weaken the supplier base, and then ask the Congress to come back and rebuild that capability and that capacity at a greater cost because when you destroy the infrastructure, it's very, very difficult to bring that back..." — Representative Rosa DeLauro

Goldman Sachs Chairman on AI and the Future of Finance | The a16z Show

a16z(273.0K)·73:45·7.3K views·2026-05-12
David Habera16z Lloyd Blankfeinformer CEO, Goldman Sachs
TL;DRTrue risk management is rooted in contingency planning rather than prediction, requiring leaders to distinguish between being "wrong" and being "reckless" while maintaining a long-term "partnership" culture that prioritizes the institution over the individual.
Prediction...

TL;DR

True risk management is rooted in contingency planning rather than prediction, requiring leaders to distinguish between being "wrong" and being "reckless" while maintaining a long-term "partnership" culture that prioritizes the institution over the individual.

The Discipline of Risk Management

  • Prediction vs. Contingency Planning: Blankfein argues that while people claim to predict the future, effective management is actually about preparing for various scenarios.
      • The "False Start" Approach: Success comes from being so prepared that you react to a "trigger" (the starting gun) faster than competitors, rather than trying to guess when the gun will go off.
      • Mitigating Costs: Insurance for a disaster is expensive when the disaster is imminent; risk management is about taking low-cost steps to mitigate adverse consequences during "quiet" times.
  • The "Mark-to-Market" Mindset: At Goldman, marking assets to market religiously served as an early warning system.
      • If a trader disputed a mark, the instruction was simple: "Sell a fraction." If the bid wasn't there, the mark was wrong.
  • Leverage and Complexity: Modern technology and AI introduce massive leverage. In the past, a mistake might be contained; today, a software error can execute 70,000 flawed transactions in seconds, creating systemic risks that are difficult to test or verify.

Leadership Under Pressure

  • Temperament in Crisis: Blankfein describes his resting state as "not resting," meaning he stays wound up normally but finds that "things slow down" during a crisis.
      • Disarming Tension: He uses humor (e.g., asking a colleague if they’ll finish their salad during an active shooter drill) to break the "freeze" of chaos and get people back to doing their jobs.
  • The Unpredictability of Resilience: You cannot tell how someone will perform in a crisis based on their "cover."
      • Example: Blankfein noted that some "man's man" types crumbled during the 2008 financial crisis, while others who seemed physically frail remained perfectly composed.
      • Board Selection: His primary advice for picking board members or leaders is to find people who have already successfully navigated a major crisis.
  • The "Socialization" of Decisions: Even in high-pressure moments, a leader should socialize tough decisions with partners. This enlists support from the "pliable" middle and honors the fact that senior staff are co-owners, not just subordinates.

Preserving a Partnership Culture

  • Partnership vs. Corporate Mindset: A partnership culture creates stability because leaders view their fortunes as tied to the whole enterprise rather than a specific silo (e.g., an Amazon retail leader rarely worries about AWS, but a Goldman partner worries about the entire firm).
  • "Long-Term Greedy": This philosophy prioritizes the firm’s reputation and long-term relationships over short-term transactional gains.
      • Commitment: During the financial crisis, Blankfein insisted on honoring lending commitments to clients like Chrysler, even when it was painful, to ensure the firm remained a trusted peer after the crisis.
  • The Role of Alumni: Blankfein established a formal alumni office to maintain lifelong ties with former employees. He emphasizes that "ex-Goldman" becomes a permanent part of an individual's identity, which reinforces the firm's global influence and recruiting power.

The Strategic Implications of AI

  • Winner-Take-All Dynamics: In finance, technology is often a "winner-take-all" game. Being milliseconds faster than a competitor can mean capturing the entire opportunity while others get nothing.
  • The "Black Box" Risk: A major concern with AI is the loss of intuition. In old trading rooms, if a price was wrong, the "shrieking" would stop because everyone's intuition caught the error. With AI, transactions happen behind the scenes without a visible "thought process" or bibliography, making errors harder to catch before they scale.
  • The Hyperscaler Advantage: Blankfein notes that current AI bets are being driven by founders (principals) putting their own "stacks of chips" on the table, which leads to more deeply held convictions—and higher stakes—than bets made by professional managers.

Advice for the Next Generation

  • Become a "Complete Person": Blankfein cautions against being too narrow or "dropping out" too early.
      • Range as Resilience: Understanding history and the humanities provides perspective. Knowing that the world has survived the Civil War, the 1960s, and the Cuban Missile Crisis (DEFCON 2) helps a leader realize that current political or economic polarization is not unprecedented.
  • Peer Influence: Your current cohort will run the world's major institutions in 30 years. Your reputation with them is being built today.
  • The Boss’s Responsibility: Leaders must realize that their subordinates go home and talk about them every night. Blankfein’s question to new managers: "What do you want them saying about you?"

***

"Most of what we do with respect to risk is not so much predicting, it’s a lot of contingency planning... What will you do if it does happen? And what can you do today to mitigate the consequences?" — Lloyd Blankfein
"Smart people tend not to do stupid things, but they tend to be wrong... It’s very important not to treat somebody who’s wrong like they’re stupid." — Lloyd Blankfein
"You’re not there to be their friend. You’re there—like a military leader—to lead them well, worry about their safety, and not make them take stupid risks for no purpose." — Lloyd Blankfein
"In the atomic age of Fukushima, if the wind had blown in a different direction, it could have been tens of millions of people... we may want to regulatorily slow some of [AI] up, not because it’s smarter than us, but because we don’t have the ability to test whether it’s right or not." — Lloyd Blankfein

Conversation with BlackRock CEO Larry Fink & Brookfield Corp CEO Bruce Flatt | Global Conference '26

Milken Institute(95.8K)·33:39·13.5K views·2026-05-12
Larry FinkCEO, BlackRock Bruce FlattCEO, Brookfield Corp
TL;DR"The United States is short power, we're short compute, we're short chips, and there's going to be shortages in all…
The global economy is undergoing a $10 trillion "rewiring" driven by AI infrastructure and energy needs, shifting investment toward massive private platforms that can match long-term liabilities with large-scale, compounding assets. Rewiring the World: Bruce Flatt...

TL;DR: The global economy is undergoing a $10 trillion "rewiring" driven by AI infrastructure and energy needs, shifting investment toward massive private platforms that can match long-term liabilities with large-scale, compounding assets.

The $10 Trillion AI Infrastructure Build-Out

  • Rewiring the World: Bruce Flatt (Brookfield) argues that we are currently in a 10-to-20-year cycle of laying the "new networks" of the global economy—AI factories, data centers, and fiber optics—paralleling the historical construction of highways and railways.
  • Supply Shortages vs. Bubbles: Larry Fink (BlackRock) asserts that there is no AI bubble; instead, there is a critical supply shortage in four areas: power, compute, chips, and memory.
      • Compute Futures: Fink predicts the emergence of a new asset class based on buying futures of compute power due to massive demand for cybersecurity and intelligence processing.
  • Capital Intensity: Individual 1-gigawatt data centers now require investments of $50 billion to $75 billion. This scale of CAPEX is increasingly being offloaded from tech "hyperscalers" (like Google or Microsoft) to private capital managers to optimize balance sheets.

Stability Through Asset-Liability Matching

  • Resilience to Redemptions: Both CEOs confirmed that less than 1% of their managed assets are redeemable on a quarterly basis. This provides a structural advantage over the banking system, which suffers from a mismatch of short-term liabilities (deposits) and long-term assets.
  • The "Compounding Machine": Bruce Flatt emphasizes that long-term success is built on earning consistent returns (roughly 12%) over decades rather than chasing volatile peaks.
      • Long-Tail Assets: Brookfield recently closed a deal where capital is held for 27 years before being returned, allowing for maximum compounding without the pressure of "churning" assets.
  • The "K Economy": Fink predicts AI will accelerate a "K-shaped" economy in every industry, creating a few dominant winners with the capital to invest in AI, while smaller firms are forced to merge.

Global Democratization of Capital Markets

  • The Retirement Shift: More countries are moving away from government-sponsored retirement systems toward self-directed, defined-contribution plans.
      • Saudi Arabia: BlackRock is working with the Kingdom to transition its retirement system toward personal accounts.
      • Japan: Fink noted that the Japanese stock market's recent surge coincided with the government doubling tax exemptions for self-directed retirement accounts (NISA).
  • Investing vs. Saving: Fink argues that keeping long-term savings in a bank account is "one of the worst financial decisions of a lifetime," as wages will not grow as fast as the potential of AI-driven capital growth.

The Consolidation of Institutional Power

  • Solutions over Products: Clients are consolidating their portfolios around fewer, larger managers who can provide "bespoke solutions" rather than just individual funds.
  • Merging Passive and Active: Fink defended the marriage of passive (iShares) and active management, noting that the ability to pivot between both—as well as between public and private markets—is what modern institutional clients demand.
  • Geopolitical Infrastructure Security: Due to the rise of drone warfare, infrastructure design is changing. Large-scale data centers and energy assets may need to be built underground or with significantly enhanced physical security to protect multi-billion dollar investments from domestic and international threats.

"The greatest miracle of investing, finance, in fact almost everything, is compounding... If you can earn north of 12% for very long periods of time, nothing else matters." — Bruce Flatt

Is Crypto Winter Over? M&A, Atkins, and Corpo Tokens

Unchained(72.7K)·65:28·144 views·2026-05-12
Katherine Kirkpatrick BosHost, Unchained Vy LeHost, Unchained Josh RiezmanChief Legal and Strategy Officer, GSR
TL;DRThe crypto industry is entering a "noisiest bear market" phase characterized by a massive surge in M&A, institutional adoption from TradFi giants, and the release of a 300-page bipartisan draft of the Clarity Act that could finally define the boundary between securities and commodities.

TL;DR

The crypto industry is entering a "noisiest bear market" phase characterized by a massive surge in M&A, institutional adoption from TradFi giants, and the release of a 300-page bipartisan draft of the Clarity Act that could finally define the boundary between securities and commodities.

Legislative Progress: The Clarity Act and Developer Protections

  • The Clarity Act Draft: A new 300-page draft of the comprehensive crypto market structure bill has been released, signaling serious bipartisan engagement in the Senate.
      • Political Hurdles: Despite compromise on yield provisions, the bill faces opposition from the bank lobby and Democrats (notably Elizabeth Warren) over ethics provisions and concerns about giving the Trump administration a political win.
      • Passage Probability: Speaker estimates vary wildly: Vy Le is highly optimistic at 90%, Josh Riezman stands at 45%, and Katherine Kirkpatrick Bos places it at 35%.
  • Blockchain Regulatory Certainty Act (BRCA): The draft includes a version of the BRCA, clarifyng that developers and non-custodial service providers are not money transmitters if they do not control user funds.
      • Specific Intent Standard: The bill introduces a "specific intent" requirement for criminal charges. This protects developers unless it can be proven they built a tool with the specific purpose of facilitating criminal activity, a higher bar than "mere knowledge" of potential abuse.

The Convergence of TradFi and Crypto Infrastructure

  • Institutional Sentiment: Josh Riezman notes that large institutions like JP Morgan, BlackRock, and Morgan Stanley are no longer skeptics but are "all in," hiring aggressively and launching products like Bitcoin ETFs.
      • Blockchain Rails: TradFi interest is shifting from the assets themselves to the underlying infrastructure to improve settlement times and trading velocity.
      • Real World Assets (RWA): GSR reports higher incoming interest regarding RWA in the last three months than in the firm's entire history.
  • Secondary Market Clarity: A primary goal of the new legislation is to distinguish the "ancillary asset" (the token) from the "investment contract" (the sale), allowing for clear secondary market trading without tokens being permanently "tainted" as securities.

"Crypto Spring" and the M&A Supercycle

  • Strategic Acquisitions: Despite anemic altcoin prices, the industry is seeing a wave of strategic M&A to fill infrastructure gaps:
      • MoonPay acquired DeFlow.
      • Bullish acquired Equiniti.
      • Kraken (Payward) acquired Reap.
  • Massive Capital Raises: Significant funding rounds indicate continued VC confidence, including new crypto-specific funds from a16z and Haun Ventures.
      • Circle’s $222M Raise: Funded by BlackRock and Apollo, valuing the company’s Ark token presale at $3 billion.
      • Public Company Tokens: Circle’s move to release the Ark L2 token suggests a "legally defensible position" that tokens can be distinct from equity, even for regulated public entities.

Regulatory Evolution: The Atkins Speech

  • Rule-making vs. Enforcement: SEC Commissioner Paul Atkins recently signaled a shift toward modernizing definitions (e.g., "exchange," "broker," "clearing agency") to fit decentralized, software-based markets rather than forcing them into 1930s-era frameworks.
  • Recognition of "Vaults": For the first time, regulators and the Clarity Act are beginning to publicly recognize "Vaults" as a legitimate decentralized infrastructure.
  • Technology Neutrality: A major upcoming challenge for regulators will be balancing the oversight of new on-chain entrants with legacy players (like the CME) to ensure neither side is unfairly disadvantaged by differing compliance costs.
"I’m calling it the noisiest bear market in history... we have bipartisan support in Congress to move forward legislation, we have real institutional engagement... and we have billions of dollars trying to find their way on chain." — Josh Riezman
"Specific intent is different than intent. Intent is just to do the act; specific intent is the mental state where you not only intend to do the act, but you intend to bring about a particular result, like intending to hit someone to murder them." — Katherine Kirkpatrick Bos
"It feels to me like the SEC and Congress have been talking this whole time because they’re in lockstep on a lot of the stuff from the legislation... modernizing securities regulation for on-chain markets through actual rule-making instead of just enforcement is the way they’re going to proceed." — Vy Le

I was so excited because he's like, this is the only pod where I don't just summarize it on AI. So shout out to you if you're listening, you know who you are. But I'm going to explain specific intent really quick for those Degens who are listening to this that aren't lawyers, as you're an important part of our audience. Specific intent is different than intent. Intent is just like intent to do the act itself, like I'm going to hit somebody. Specific intent is the mental state where you not only intend to hit somebody but you also intend to bring about a particular result. So like for example, I intend to hit you to murder you. That's specific intent to kill. So you can see the distinction between general intent and specific intent—you want to bring about a certain act. This term seems kind of nerdy and niche but it's actually really important for developer protections. Yeah. It's also important to distinguish specific intent from mere knowledge, right? So like it's possible that you could create some sort of privacy tool for legitimate purposes, knowing that maybe it could be abused by bad actors. But that is not enough. You need specific intent to want them to do that. That must be the purpose for creating it, right? So that's actually I think a really important distinction, because I think a lot of the developers of these tools would say we didn't create this because we wanted bad actors to use them, we created it because people legitimately need privacy tools for transactions. And if the prosecution can't prove that that's not true, then they won't meet that element and so that's the other reason it's really important to have that added. Yeah, yeah totally.
