What women’s sports reveals about building future leaders: Deloitte’s Lara Abrash

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·23:39·266 views·2026-05-12
Lara AbrashChair, Deloitte US
TL;DRInvesting in women’s sports is a high-yield economic opportunity and a critical talent pipeline for future executives, as 85% of women in leadership positions credit sports for their professional success.
Rapid Market Growth: Revenue from women’s sports is projected to...

TL;DR

Investing in women’s sports is a high-yield economic opportunity and a critical talent pipeline for future executives, as 85% of women in leadership positions credit sports for their professional success.

The Economic Case for Women’s Sports

  • Rapid Market Growth: Revenue from women’s sports is projected to grow from $2.5 billion to over $3 billion by 2026, consistently seeing double-digit annual growth.
  • The Investment Gap: Despite high interest, women’s sports currently receive only 10% of sponsorship dollars and 15% of media coverage.
  • Redefining ROI: Lara Abrash argues that traditional viewership metrics are insufficient.
      • Broadened KPIs: Investors should look at high social media engagement, merchandising opportunities, and the outsized influence of female athletes as icons.
      • The Vicious Cycle: Lack of broadcast visibility limits advertising dollars; breaking this cycle requires "pioneer" investors to fund the visibility needed to unlock full market potential.

The Leadership Pipeline: "Girls Who Play Become Women Who Lead"

  • Executive Success: A Deloitte survey found that 85% of women in executive positions attribute their success to lessons learned in sports, with over 90% stating sports helped them reach higher career levels.
  • Skill Cultivation: Sports uniquely prepare women for business by fostering specific mindsets:
      • Grit: Defined by Abrash as the resilience developed through "falling and hitting things" and powering through moments when things aren't going well.
      • The Team Mosaic: Sports teach that a leader does not need to be a "superwoman" with all the answers; rather, they must be the person who can tap into the best version of every team member.
      • Manager vs. Leader: Abrash notes that "managers manage outcome, leaders lead people." Sports teach selflessness and the ability to untap the potential of those around you.

Barriers to Entry and Participation

  • The Dropout Rate: 45% of girls leave sports due to body confidence issues. For girls from lower-income households, the barriers to participation are six times higher.
  • Opportunity Gap: Girls have 1.3 million fewer opportunities to participate in sports than boys, exacerbated by cultural norms and physical/mental pressures.
  • Declining Role Models: In 1972, a significantly higher percentage of coaches were women; today, only 40% of coaches are women, which negatively impacts the confidence of young female athletes who lack relatable role models.
  • Professional Sustainability: Over 75% of professional female athletes make less than $50,000 annually, making the career path economically unsustainable for many.

Strategic Priorities for Global Leaders

  • Investment as an Engine: Leaders should re-evaluate marketing and social purpose budgets to find opportunities to invest in women’s leagues and teams now, while the market is at a "precipice" of massive expansion.
  • Internal Talent Audits: Organizations should look at their next generation of female leaders and specifically harness the skills of athletes already within their ranks.
  • Long-term Vision: Abrash predicts that in 20–30 years, successful investment will lead to a world where gender parity in sports is the norm, creating a massive influx of women positioned for CEO and board chair roles.

Notable Quotes

"Girls who play become women who lead. Our survey has told us that 85% of women who are in executive positions talk about the lessons they learned from sports as being major drivers to why they're so successful." — Lara Abrash
"Managers manage outcome, leaders lead people. And that's ultimately what sports teaches you to do. It teaches you to lead people. Being a teammate to someone and helping them be their best version and being selfless... that's ultimately what leaders end up doing." — Lara Abrash
"This is not something we're doing because it's a nice thing to do or the right thing to do; we're actually doing it because it actually will create great outsized ROI." — Lara Abrash
We're at the precipice of things that hold people back. I think we're just about to overcome it. Welcome to Meet the Leader, the podcast where top leaders share how they're tackling the world's toughest challenges. In today's episode, we dig into an unsung strategy to bridge gender gaps and drive economic growth: women's sports. Subscribe to Meet the Leader on Apple, Spotify, and wherever you get your favorite podcasts. And don't forget to rate and review us. I'm Linda Lacina from the World Economic Forum, and this is Meet the Leader. 85% of women who are in executive positions talk about the lessons they learned from sports as being major drivers to why they're so successful. We all know that sports is a big business, but you might not realize that it comprises 2% of global GDP and that it's poised to more than double by 2030, becoming an 8.8 trillion dollar market by 2050. That makes it a multiplier for change. As our Sports for People and Planet report points out, that growth will be an engine for tourism, for city development, for infrastructure, and can even help tackle health and obesity. To help drive that growth, we can't overlook women's sports. While some projections have that market tripling in the short term, Lara Abrash will remind us that the opportunity is bigger than just a single match or a single game. Support for women's sports helps develop leadership and cooperation, and that's critical for bridging the gender gap and women's longevity in their careers. Lara, the chair of Deloitte US—Deloitte being one of the largest professional services firms in the world—will share why investing in women's sports makes economic sense. But she'll also share her personal journey with sports and how it shaped her as a leader and team member, and what anyone can learn. Well, we're definitely in a moment when it comes to women's sports. For several years we've been talking about this emergence of women's sports, but we are now going from nearly two and a half billion dollars of global revenue to ultimately over three billion dollars in '26, and each and every year we're seeing double-digit growth. And for me as a business person, I've been doing business for over 30 years. When you see momentum of each year having double-digit growth, we're now scaled into a moment of being able to try to scale this even greater into hopefully a place where we're starting to near what men's sports have looked like, which also took decades and decades to get many of those sports to where they are today. Now there's still, amazingly, an investment gap. Can you tell us a little bit about that? What is it and why does it exist? Yeah, there's a really big gap when it comes to women's sports. Ultimately, a lot of organizations are not necessarily seeing the ROI in the way they've necessarily saw men's sports. So the investment dollars are not going in the way you've historically seen in men's sports. And even though there's a huge excitement and interest in women's sports, we're not seeing the dollars necessarily follow through. We ultimately are seeing the ROI lagging, because ROI does come from, you know, how many eyes are on the screen. And when we're not getting displayed at the same level as men's sports, you're not going to see the ROI. But the reality is we now need to think about ROI differently when it comes to women's sports, because the interest is incredibly high. The merchandising opportunities are incredibly high, and the engagement with the leaders, the athletes themselves, is very different from men's sports. There's a huge interest in women as influencers, as icons, and people that go beyond the field. They want to hear about their stories and the engagement on social media. So we have to think about this ROI greater, but ultimately, there's a huge opportunity for return on investment to get in now to be part of the future. It's not just the right thing to do; it truly is an investment opportunity. There were some statistics I found. Women's sports get just 10% of the sponsorship dollars, 15% of the media coverage, and of course just a fraction of things like broadcast right fees and things like that. What is holding people back? What are they saying anyway? Well, they're saying that, again, they're not necessarily seeing the same type of return on investment that they see, but it ultimately becomes this vicious cycle. If you don't display the sports on the TV to get the advertising dollars and to get the viewership eyes, you're not going to necessarily see the ROI come in that way. So we have to start putting in more money to get them displayed. Ultimately, that's what's going to lead to the path. It requires people seeing this interest, and we're really there—we are truly there right now—that investment should be going in and you will start to see us get over that hump. So it's about getting viewership rights and people seeing the broader KPIs that come from women's sports. As you've been digging into this topic, what has surprised you? Where have you been like, "Oh gosh," what's taken you back? Well, what surprises me most about the data I'm seeing is, ultimately, I think about women's sports in three life cycle elements. There's the entrance into the sport, so girls coming in and then staying in sport, then becoming professionals and ultimately really burgeoning the opportunity, and then leaving sport into leadership. When we talk about entering sport, one of the things that surprised me is how hard it is for girls to want to stay in women's sports. 45% of girls do not stay in sport; they leave because of body confidence issues. And when you look at that data and you think about girls coming from economic households that are lower, it's six times even more. The opportunities for girls to participate in sport is 1.3 million times fewer than men's sports. So this ability for girls to just get started and stay is so much lower and there're so many barriers. There are the physical views about it, the mental views about it, and the cultural norms about girls playing sports. And if they're not going to even get into it and stay with it, then the opportunities to be a professional and the opportunities to take sport into life just become even harder. I read Deloitte had done its own survey that found a connection between women leaders and sort of a background in sports. It prepares them for teamwork but also to sort of drive things. What do you think about that? Of course. We have this saying at the firm: "Girls who play become women who lead." And we have data behind it. Our survey has told us that 85% of women who are in executive positions talk about the lessons they learned from sports as being major drivers to why they're so successful. Over 90% of them would say that they're in an even higher level because of sport. And I myself have had that benefit, and I see that with our clients and our peers. It's in government, it's in business, it's in almost every facet of life. The more a girl can play a sport, the more she's going to gain some of these lessons that will help her later in life. How about just keeping girls in sports? We can start there. What's needed to fix that? Oh, so many things. So for a girl to want to be staying in sports, it's the cultural norms and it's the role modeling. One really interesting data point is over the last 40 or 50 years, we've actually seen a decline in the number of women coaches. Back in 1972 we had a huge number of women coaching, but now only 40% of coaches are women. And when you think about the confidence to actually want to be a player, you want to look up and look at role models that look like you, that make you feel more comfortable. So the lack of women coaching is one element. The other element I talked earlier about is the cultural norms. We need to encourage these girls to want to stay. We need to encourage them to understand that it's okay for women to have a different body size to be successful in sport, but these body image things are starting to be a reason to hold them back. So it's the cultural norms, it's the coaching, and the role modeling. That will start to settle hopefully girls wanting to enter and stay. And what about elsewhere within professional sports? Are there certain strategic investments that we need to be making, maybe either to the pipeline or also to the structure of some of these organizations—these affiliate organizations and things like that? What strategic investment should we be thinking about first? Well, we need to invest into the leagues and we need to be investing into the teams so that they could start to pay their athletes at the right levels. A really interesting data point we found is that over three quarters of the women who play sports professionally make less than fifty thousand dollars US. I mean, that is not going to allow somebody to raise a family or to live their life. So ultimately, we have to invest into these organizations so that they can pay the athletes. The more they do that, there'll be more interest in girls actually wanting to be in sport and stay in sport, which ultimately when they leave sport will help society because we'll have more women leaders. What is needed maybe to change the mindset? How does that work? So to change the mindset of who wants to invest into it is, as I said earlier, really having them understand the real return that we'll get. The ability to get more viewership and to get more women on TV playing sports will create a greater ROI. So we need to be able to change the awareness, but ultimately understand that there are other KPIs than just viewership as a way to understand how women's sports creates an ROI. As I said earlier, you actually have an ability to have deeper, deeper, deeper engagement with the athletes, which creates outsized merchandising opportunities. So when you look at the whole ecosystem of what a women's sport brings, it brings much greater ROI than just what you would naturally see for TV ads. So we have to invest in the leagues, we have to invest into getting more opportunities for media, and more opportunities for viewership. I think it's interesting. You mentioned that, hey, you know, even the NFL had to build to where it is. We've seen this growth with other sports; we've seen it with soccer. Soccer was something that maybe wasn't being watched 20 or 30 years ago like it is today. Why do people struggle sometimes to see the potential of something, to see something that hasn't existed yet? There's a lot of reasons we struggle. One reason is ROI, you know, there's limited capital. So when organizations are thinking about where to put their dollars down, they can invest back in their employees, they can invest in technology, AI. This idea of investing in branding and investing into the opportunities to get an ROI through women's sports is something different, and there's a natural cultural norm to have this hesitation. Is this going to generate it? And it requires quite frankly pioneers. You mentioned the women's soccer—huge pioneers to actually get that to the championship game on at night. It was a Herculean effort to do that, but ultimately the response by the viewers was overwhelming. The, you know, women's soccer actually gets greater viewership than men's soccer does, but ultimately was not getting the same opportunity. So it takes real pioneers. Billie Jean King did it for tennis, and she actually continues to do it today for a lot of these other teams. Now you're starting to see investors invest in new sports. We now have a women's hockey league, we have women's baseball, and you're starting to see this expansion. I actually think we're at the precipice of some of the things that hold people back. I think we're just about to overcome it. Is there a change story that you can kind of point to that really shows that before and after, where there was an investment and it really paid off and it showed kind of the direction we could be going in? Well, if I think about where we are in 2025 going into 2026, one of the things I think about is I took the Olympics as a start. The Olympics for the first time ever had gender parity, where you had the same number of women athletes as men athletes. And for the first time we had a women's rugby story. And because of some of the athletes themselves, the excitement and the interest now in women's rugby has changed significantly just from the Olympics having parity to just an amazing year of women's rugby. And now there's an interest in that sport outside of the Olympics in a much greater way. That's an example of that dynamic of the athletes themselves and the attention they can bring. They're influencers, they're on social media, they're doing podcasts, and the interest in their stories is so much greater, as is their interest in the sport. If we get this right—like if we get women's sports investment right—what does the world look like in say 20 years? What could we expect to see? If we get women's sports right and the investment right, I think there's two or three things that'll happen. One, I believe to start, because this is the most important thing, that actually will generate real business and money for society and for business leaders. This is not something we're doing because it's a nice thing to do or the right thing to do; we're actually doing it because it actually will create great outsized ROI. That's one. Two, I believe we will have even greater women leaders in the future. The more women we have in sport, exiting sport into executive roles and business roles, you name it, we're going to have more. And the thing that excites me the most is the girls themselves will look up and they'll say, "They did it." So I think in 20 or 30 years it won't be a question about body image and "do I have a woman coach." I'll just want to play. I think we'll have a huge ROI and then ultimately leaders will be coming out in droves because they played sport. We'll have not just a greater number of women leaders, but whether they're sitting in CEO seats, board chair seats, or senior roles in the government, there'll be more women that could be positioned to take on these roles. And as they leverage the skills they build from playing sports for all those years, they're going to be better positioned to be successful in those roles. I want to talk a little bit about sort of what drives you and inspires you, and maybe the role that sport has played in your life. You were the only girl on your baseball team as a child? Yes. When I started to play sports, I had three older brothers. So my mom, of course, with the fourth was like, "Okay, you've got to go play sports also." And there was no softball team in my town. There was no girls' baseball team. So when I grew up, the only way to play sport, to play baseball, was actually to be on the boys' baseball team. So yeah, I was the only girl on a boys' baseball team, and that's how I started and that's all I know. I joke that I played the position "left out," but ultimately it was a great experience and that was normal back then. Today that's not as normal, but back then that's just the way it was. How does that prepare you? Yeah, sports has been huge for me. I could talk all day about the things I learned. I'll pick a few things. One, I often talk about grit. Grit is the part of your elbow that's rough that you get over time from falling and hitting things. That's what grit is. And grit ultimately is the thing that shows up when things aren't going well and powers you through the really tough moments. And if you've played sport, and being on a losing team and those hard days or practice when things don't go right, or when you show up and the team doesn't pull it all together, you lose. And that year you're just building that grit on you. And that's something that I benefited from. I played baseball, I played tennis, I played basketball—a lot of different sports. And ultimately all those experiences helped me in ways that I sometimes don't even think about. Another area that sports has been great for me has been the knowledge that it's okay to not be the best person on the team. Ultimately, it's about bringing the team together. And as a leader in business, you're not necessarily there because you're the best person at whatever your company does. You're the best person who can tap into what every person can bring—the best version of themselves. And that's what a team is. That's what whatever sports team you play on, it's about bringing that person in to play their position in a certain way and to contribute to the team. And there're so many other things: communication, teamwork and the like. All of that comes and it builds a girl's confidence. And when confidence gets built, you don't have to have someone in the back of your head telling you how great you are; you start to realize I can do this. And when you get to those hard days at work, you have that confidence with you. There's a thing when you're working together on the field, where you learn that you can solve problems together. We don't need any one person to be a superwoman. Why is that shift? How does sports help women sort of overcome the superwoman fallacy? Yeah, so as you probably know for years, for women in business, there has been this idea that you have to be this superwoman; you have to be the person who could do everything. And quite frankly, you've had a lot of people fearing that women will go cry if they don't get what they want at work, and so they have to put on this veneer that they have all the answers. What sports teaches everybody is it ultimately does take a team. It's a mosaic. It's people of different colors and shapes and sizes coming together to solve an issue. And it's helped women realize that they don't have to declare that they know everything. It actually does, in fact, take a village. And so the woman who could actually bring the team together—and I would argue that's actually one of the superpowers that many women have—is the ability to nurture and engage others to figure out how do I get the best out of this person on this particular day and this particular moment. That's what women do. And when they start to realize that that is the power that they can unleash, it's not about them being the superwoman, but ultimately them leading. I have another saying which is: "Managers manage outcome, leaders lead people." And that's ultimately what sports teaches you to do. It teaches you to lead people. Being a teammate to someone and helping them be their best version and being selfless—giving them the ball, having them take the shot and kick the goal. That's ultimately what leaders end up doing. They're not the best person in the organization; they're the person who could untap everybody around them. Is there a lesson maybe from a coach or maybe from sports in general that shaped you? Yeah, probably one of the best lessons I ever got from a coach was there's never enough practice. You cannot know what life is going to put in front of you. I used to have a coach who would take me out—Coach Catalano. He would take me out after school, and whenever all the girls left the field we would continue. I played center field at that time, and we would continue to just flag fly balls. And then in a game, I found myself doing something that we had covered that we may not have gotten to. It was like this idea that practice does in fact make perfection. And so you are constantly pushing yourself. This coach told me, when you actually get to a point that you think you're trying to be perfect, you haven't actually pushed yourself enough. So this idea of "practice does make perfection" is something this coach taught me and he made me limit. And to this day, I try to practice things inside and outside of work, just for that moment when it may come up. Was there maybe a moment when you were playing sports that maybe helps you even today, either as a leader or even as a teammate? Is there something that's like, "Gosh, you still use that now"? Well, definitely when I was in sports, I was the person who tried to cheer everybody up. I'm still—I play on a team today. I actually play on an over-25 women's softball team. I joke it's way over 25. And on that team I'm the manager, despite my big job at work, because I'm the person who really does try to encourage and support and excite everybody around us. And I think for me it started to make me realize that, one, I had a natural talent in getting people around me to want to perform and to push themselves. And it ultimately taught me that I was more valuable doing that than being the center fielder. And that's what I try to bring to work today—that me being the best accountant or consultant is not why I'm here. I'm ultimately here because I can get the people around me excited and maybe to push themselves in ways that they didn't think. And that ultimately really did come from the softball field and still does. Are there things that we can learn from athletes about effective teaming, especially maybe women who didn't have the chance to play sports? Like what are questions they could ask or things they should look for to be even better at teaming? I think from a teaming perspective, women really could help each other. I think we're a superpower when we come together. I do find in business sometimes women are, again, thinking they have to be the person who knows everything and show no vulnerability. And ultimately I'm seeing today women who show more vulnerability and are more open to helping each other, being helped, and being open that they need help. They start to create better teams around them. And I think that's something that if you hadn't played sports, you're going to have to realize through experience that it is in fact helping you, but it's not a natural thing. I still find in business there is a little bit of competition among women, and it really is a village. When the team succeeds, the person succeeds. Yeah. Is there a book you recommend? Oh, probably *All In* by Billie Jean King. I think she's amazing. How did it change you? Well, she changes me because she just is so inspiring as an athlete and as a leader. She makes me want to rise to the biggest moments and realize that I have an ability to pay it forward and give back, and you have to in fact be all in. You have to give everything to do it, and incrementalism is only going to get you so far. So she ultimately has pushed me to be more about giving all of me and not a piece of me, and if I give all of me, bigger things could happen. What should leaders be prioritizing in 2026? I think leaders should be thinking about—particularly in women's sports—that this is the time right now to find opportunities to invest into the sport. Go back to our organizations and look at our marketing dollars, our social purpose dollars that we're spending, and look at opportunities to get women into investing into the sports. Then look at your own organization and start to say: "Where are my next set of generation of women leaders, and am I tapping into the athletes in my organization and the skills that they've learned, and how do I start to harness that?" So I think there's great opportunity to be the engine to invest right now into women's sports, to engage into it, and then ultimately to harness what is inside your own organization relative to women's leadership. That was Lara Abrash. Thanks so much to her. And thanks so much to you for listening. If there's someone in your life looking to learn more about teaming and cooperation, send them this episode. Find a transcript of this episode, as well as transcripts from my colleague's podcast, Radio Davos, at wef.ch/podcasts. To find our recent Sports for People and Planet report, go to the show notes of this episode. We'll make sure to have a link. That's it for now. I'm Linda Lacina from the World Economic Forum. Have a great day.

Rethink Everything: AWS Financial Services 2026 New York City Symposium Keynote

AWS Events(176.0K)·72:26·56 views·2026-05-12
Scott MullinsManaging Director of Financial Services, AWS
TL;DRThe financial services industry has entered the "agentic era," shifting from generative AI assistants to autonomous agents that can build software, modernize legacy systems, and conduct micro-transactions.
AWS is positioning itself as the foundational infrastructure for...

TL;DR

The financial services industry has entered the "agentic era," shifting from generative AI assistants to autonomous agents that can build software, modernize legacy systems, and conduct micro-transactions. AWS is positioning itself as the foundational infrastructure for this transition, prioritizing model choice and specialized tools like AgentCore Payments to bridge the gap between human reasoning and autonomous execution.

I. Accelerating the Software Development Lifecycle (SDLC)

AWS and its partners are moving beyond "AI widgets" toward AI-native engineering where agents handle the heavy lifting of coding, testing, and documentation.

  • DTCC Case Study: Dr. Johnna Powell highlighted DTCC’s transition to an AI-native enterprise using "DTCC IQ," a logically unified AI ecosystem.
      • Tooling Success: After evaluating five tools, DTCC selected Amazon Q Developer, achieving an 85% daily adoption rate among its 1,800 engineers.
      • Productivity Gains: The firm saw a 40% improvement in productivity for daily users; specifically, Java upgrades that previously took 100 days were completed in just 10 days.
      • Future Metrics: DTCC is targeting a 25% increase in code commits and raising test coverage requirements to 85% by the end of 2026.
  • Kiro (AI-Powered IDE): Scott Mullins emphasized Kiro’s ability to turn natural language prompts into production-ready code, documentation, and tests, democratizing the "builder" role for non-technical knowledge workers.
  • Model Choice: AWS reaffirmed its commitment to flexibility, noting that by 2028, most enterprises will use over 24 different AI models. Key updates include:
      • Anthropic: Direct access to the full Claude console via AWS accounts.
      • OpenAI: Expansion of the partnership to bring OpenAI models, the Codex coding agent, and managed agents to Amazon Bedrock.

II. Modernizing Legacy Infrastructure

AI is now the primary catalyst for migrating mission-critical applications that were previously considered too complex or expensive to move to the cloud.

  • The Hartford’s Cloud-Native Pivot: Raghu Parthasarathy detailed the insurer’s goal to exit its production data centers by late 2026.
      • Modernization Scoring: The company uses a 1-to-5 scale to prioritize "cloud-native" transformations (Postgres/Aurora) over "lift-and-shift" (limited to 10% of assets).
      • Efficiency: Using AWS Transform, The Hartford reduced its development lifecycle by 20% and successfully moved .NET applications to Linux platforms.
  • Legacy Transformation at Scale:
      • Experian: Modernized seven legacy .NET applications, saving 300 engineering days and automating the transformation of nearly 690,000 lines of code.
      • Itaú Unibanco: Utilized AI to extract business logic from mainframe systems that lacked formal documentation, reducing testing efforts by 30%.
  • Threat Prevention: Parthasarathy noted that as deployment speed increases, the security window has shrunk. Threat prevention must now occur within 20 minutes to counter infiltration-to-exfiltration cycles that have dropped from days to just 72 minutes.

III. Reimagining Customer and Employee Experiences

Financial institutions are utilizing Amazon Connect Customer to strike a balance between autonomous self-service and high-touch human interaction.

  • U.S. Bank’s Omnichannel Strategy: Corporate CIO Andy Bingenheimer explained the move from 100+ disparate contact centers to a single platform handling 15 million calls per month.
      • Scalability: During the Union Bank acquisition, the platform scaled effortlessly to handle a 15% volume increase over a single weekend.
      • Agentic Future: The bank is moving from a "reactive" model to a "proactive/autonomous" one, where agents anticipate customer needs based on spending patterns and provide seamless context sharing across mobile, phone, and branch channels.
  • Operational Impacts:
      • Canada Life: Achieved a 94% reduction in wait times and a 10% reduction in average handle time within six months.
      • Robinhood: Deployed generative AI support agents on Amazon Bedrock that resolve 65% of customer queries without human intervention.

IV. The Next Frontier: Agentic Commerce

A major barrier to fully autonomous agents has been their inability to cross paywalls or license-metered APIs. AWS announced a solution to integrate financial transactions directly into the AI execution loop.

  • Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Payments: A new capability (in preview) that allows agents to autonomously pay for APIs, proprietary data, and services.
  • X-402 Protocol: This HTTP-native protocol enables instant stablecoin micro-payments (fractions of a cent) without human intervention.
  • Governance & Guardrails:
      • Deterministic Controls: Spending limits are enforced at the infrastructure layer (per session or user) rather than being left to the agent's judgment.
      • Partnerships: Launch partners include Coinbase (wallet/facilitation) and Stripe (wallet support).
  • Universal Utility: Beyond developer APIs, the vision includes agents performing commercial transactions like booking flights or purchasing research reports directly through the execution loop.
"The main difference [between the introduction of electricity and AI] is that it took electric light 40 years to reorganize every industry on earth, whereas today’s technological changes are moving much, much, much faster." — Scott Mullins, AWS
"The shift from suggestion to commit marks a fundamental change in software development... a move from AI as a supportive assistant to AI as an accountable agent." — Dr. Johnna Powell, DTCC
"We recognize that we are competing with one another... but we’re also competing with that last great experience our customer had, regardless of whether it was with their bank or another organization." — Andy Bingenheimer, U.S. Bank
"Today when an agent hits a paywall, it stops. Not because it cannot reason through the task, but it has no way to pay... We see a future where billions of agents are autonomously making micro-transactions." — Preethi, Amazon Bedrock AgentCore
In Financial Services, the idea of "good enough" has never been good enough. So how do we keep getting better? By working smarter with Amazon Quick. By building and deploying faster with Kiro. By scaling agents with Amazon Bedrock AgentCore. By personalizing every customer experience with Amazon Connect Customer. With these capabilities, now we can rethink how we work, rethink how we collaborate, rethink how we build, rethink how we shop, rethink how we save, rethink how we protect, rethink how we grow, rethink how we invest, rethink how we connect, rethink how we share, rethink insurance, rethink personalization, rethink settlement, and rethink everything. Welcome to the 2026 Symposium. Please welcome to the stage, Managing Director, Worldwide Financial Services, Amazon Web Services, Scott Mullins. Good morning, and welcome to the 2026 AWS Financial Services Symposium right here in New York City. I'm Scott Mullins and it's my privilege to lead the Financial Services organization at AWS, and more importantly to serve as the host for today's event. We are delighted that you have chosen to invest your day with us, and we're determined to ensure that you get a good return on that investment. Now, looking at the video that we played just now, you might have found yourself chuckling when you saw some of the innovations that have changed the way that the financial services industry works over the previous decades. As the industry has progressed in line with consumer demands, more and more you've been able to turn what was once thought extraordinary into ordinary. For example, we may not think of check writing as all that innovative anymore because, well, we all hate writing checks today, or we've just stopped doing it altogether. But the substitution of a piece of paper for physical cash represented a major step in the expansion of the financial system. And as hard as I try, I still have to order a checkbook every four or five years because there's one or two vendors that want a check. Today when we think about innovation in our industry, what's different is that new technology isn't just driving incremental change in one particular area, like trading or payments. Instead, it's impacting every corner of the industry, all at once, which is making it so much easier to turn new ideas into actions as quickly as possible. New technologies are driving us to think differently, but they're also allowing us to act differently, which is driving a fundamental organizational reorganization of our industry. This is comparable to the introduction of electric light in the late 1800s. When Thomas Edison established the Pearl Street Station power plant in 1882, not too far from here where we are today in the financial district, electricity wasn't just a better way to light a room, it represented a way to reorganize every single industry on earth. The main difference here is that it took electric light 40 years to do it, whereas today's technological changes are moving much, much, much faster. And we're seeing this speed today with financial institutions across every market segment and in every region reinventing themselves with AWS. Nowhere are we seeing faster speed of adoption than with AI. AWS is the infrastructure powering new forms of intelligence. We've put advanced capabilities, including sophisticated AI agents and the choice of industry-leading models, within reach of every single AWS customer, and not just the largest players. And the industry is seizing the opportunity to adapt these new capabilities. All the customers that you see on the slides behind me represent but a fraction of the financial institutions who are running agents on AWS today. And that progress has begun to change the industry in some really profound ways. For instance, let's take investment research. The complexity and duration of tasks that you can solve with agentic AI is growing exponentially while the cost to do so is plummeting. And that signifies a paradigm shift for research. Now, you can assign different agents working under a supervisory agent to access a range of data sources, to spin up multiple models and run them in parallel. Analyzing data, both structured and unstructured, is so much easier with natural language interfaces and visualization. You don't have to be a trained data scientist anymore to test sophisticated hypotheses. Research agents can even propose novel strategies and generate ideas for investors and not just execute simple tasks. New technologies are democratizing advanced capabilities while offloading undifferentiated heavy lifting. And this is a pattern we're seeing across the industry. Underwriting and lending are becoming much more automated. Financial advice is becoming more proactive, instead of reactive. Customer service is becoming more personalized. And payments are moving autonomously between machines, instead of between humans and businesses. And we're going to hear more about that right after this session. Now, it's not that the financial services industry has been slow to harness new technology to evolve. It's that today, new technology is changing how far we can go and, more importantly, how fast we can go. The industry now finds itself at a bit of a crossroads. We've come a long way, but how do we keep getting better and better at serving financial consumers? Well, first, getting better requires us to ask ourselves some hard questions. To make sure that we're thinking about AI strategically, and we're not just bolting it onto the side of existing products or processes like a widget. That approach will not yield the return on investment that organizations require. So we need to ask ourselves questions like: Do we have the right governance structure to scale AI responsibly? Is our data estate ready for all of this? Do we have the ability to orchestrate our agents or are we going to keep running them in silos? How are we evolving software development? Is our workforce ready for the way that work works now and the way it will work in the future? Now, some of these questions are technical in nature, but most of them are not. Because implementing AI across the enterprise isn't a purely technical effort. It includes people and process changes that are fundamental to how companies operate. And it's critical to ask these questions because we won't get anywhere we want to go by doing things the way that we've always done them. You can't build tomorrow's applications and products on yesterday's dogma and legacy technology. When it comes to transforming ideas into products, we've created multiple pathways to help you succeed, based on your specific needs, resources and, most importantly, your goals. You can buy ready-made agents, you can build your own custom agents, or you can work with experts, including AWS partners or the AWS Generative AI Innovation Center to develop brand-new agents. And in terms of turnkey solutions, AWS is helping organizations compress the gap between thinking and building. These are just some of our more recent launches, and you can see that they cover a wide spectrum of capabilities, running from product development, operations, to security and then all the way to customer experience. Today, we're going to hear from customers on how they are using these and other tools to do what they couldn't do before, or to do things in a much more efficient and effective way. One of these tools that customers are really excited about is Kiro. Kiro is our AI-powered IDE that we launched last year and it's allowing organizations to rethink what they can build, how they can build, and who can be a builder. Kiro makes it much easier and faster to move from concept to production. It turns natural language prompts into detailed specs and then into working code, documentation and tests. You can build new proofs of concept quickly without sacrificing production readiness, and iteration is easy so that you can build exactly what you envisioned. Kiro enables thinkers to become builders. It provides access to skills that were previously out of reach for folks who lack deep technical expertise. And for that reason, I believe that Kiro is going to unleash a new wave of creativity across the industry as we turn more knowledge workers into builders. I think we probably looked at five or six enterprise tools. Amazon Q Developer was selected. Probably most of you are aware, it's a Gen AI-powered assistant for building and operating and transforming software. It assists engineers with a variety of activities across the software delivery lifecycle, and runs natively on AWS with access to DTCC code repositories. We selected Q based on superior security and privacy scoring, that's paramount at DTCC, and also customization ability for DTCC repos, and the viability of future tool evolution, and also of course our investment with an established partner such as Amazon. And after selection of that tool, our focus was really very structured around rolling out education to empower our engineering teams. So we established a 14-week training program initially. This was focused on our application development and testing engineers. And we exceeded our goal to train about over 80% of the cohort in the first quarter of 2025. Training included, and this is just really important because in order to drive adoption, you really have to dig deep into the training programs. We established a Center of Excellence with ambassadors to drive transparency across the IT organization as capabilities evolved. And we also had super users that were embedded in squads to drive adoption in teams. After the initial rollout, we expanded the training to focus on infrastructure engineering and the broader community. So with the tool now in the engineers' hands for 12 months, we garnered useful insights on adoption and productivity alongside some key lessons. Approximately 1800 engineers, or 85% of them, use Q daily. From a productivity perspective, we've observed consistent about 40% improvement in productivity from daily users compared to infrequent users. And we also observed lots of improvements from historically manual engineering tasks. So, for example, what used to take Java upgrades, let's say 100 days, now takes 10 days. It's essentially 10% of the time that it used to take. So we also, we noticed a lot of positive things but also, we took away some, some insights. So it was fantastic to empower teams to innovate on solutions. But we also, you know, realized there were a lot of similar prompts being created across squads. There's an opportunity to kind of consolidate and get common tools across the board. And we also needed to invest in MCP in order to increase context. So that moves us to, essentially our SDLC AI factory. The shift from suggestion to commit really marks a fundamental change in software development, and our operating model. And this signals a move from AI as a supportive assistant to AI as an accountable agent. And we're executing this transition deliberately through our AI SDLC factory depicted here, to sustain adoption momentum while reshaping governance, controls and also productivity measurement. The SDLC factory is DTCC's structured initiative to operationalize agentic AI across the software lifecycle while maintaining human in the loop, at appropriate stages for control. It has a number of features including MCP infrastructure. Kiro also enables significant augmentation of software development which enables agentic SDLC by contributing to code generation, test creation, documentation and much, much more. We leverage AWS Bedrock, AgentCore, which powers multi-agent orchestration and of course governance is foundational. And so we also, let me move to our impact. So this is obviously key to ensuring that we're measuring performance in an objective way. Our approach to measuring AI impact in software delivery intentionally follows a progressive maturity model. In 2026, we're actually transitioning from adoption to productivity measures with insights at the individual engineering level. And that's enabled through a tool called Jellyfish. And as AI evolves from just assistive tooling to more delegated execution, our focus is going to be on the control plane foundations. So a couple of metrics that we're consistently measuring on a frequent basis include productivity. So we're looking at the increase of the average number of code commits by developer by 25%, that's our target. Quality: we're increasing test code coverage requirements to 85%. Another quality measure is to reduce open defect aging target to 15%, and along automation we're increasing test automation metrics for modernization to 93% and we also are looking at velocity. We want to improve squad throughput by 25%. So all in all, our partnership with AWS has been just phenomenal in terms of enabling us to achieve all of these targets and we're very excited about our measurable trajectory forward and our deep partnership with AWS in enabling our success. Legacy processes and systems should not hinder any of our ambitions. We've reached the point where financial institutions no longer need to devote resources to maintaining systems that aren't allowing them to innovate on behalf of their customers. And what's especially exciting is that the range of different modernization patterns that financial institutions can now pursue is expanding every day. Now, there are only about 260 working days in any given year. And so if any of you were told that you could save a full year of one person's work, I imagine you would take somebody up on that offer. AWS Transform's agentic capabilities help automate 687,600 lines of code transformation. This improves application performance across the board while freeing experienced engineers teams to focus on higher value projects. Itaú Unibanco, Latin America's largest bank, also turned to AI to help modernize its technology platform, so that it could better serve customers in a highly competitive market. Looking at our monolithic applications, giving them the microservices architecture if you will. Let's talk about capabilities, Preethi. Good morning everyone. I'm Preethi and I lead the product and engineering for Amazon Bedrock AgentCore and the foundation of AWS agentic AI portfolio. I'm excited to be here today not just to share what we are building, but how we can shape this agentic future together. As Scott just showed us, agentic AI isn't just changing financial services. It is also helping us think about how to act on those ideas and transform the entire industry. And that's exactly where we are placing our biggest bets at AWS here. Over the past few years, we have seen agents evolve from simple rule-based generative AI assistants that simply responded to prompts to goal-driven agents capable of multi-step reasoning and task completion. And to now, fully autonomous agents that can execute complex workflows, make decisions, and collaborate with little to no human oversight. The number of resources these agents can use is also growing every week. Agents aren't using a single tool anymore. They are dynamically discovering and composing dozens of tools per task, and they are doing this autonomously. What makes this possible is reasoning. Reasoning is what elevates an agent from simple tool executors to intelligent decision makers, giving them the capability to break down complex tasks, select the right set of tools and sequence them, and continuously adapt to new information. But reasoning alone is not enough. What you need is purpose-built agentic infrastructure that helps these agents operate at scale, securely, reliably, and with full accountability. That's exactly why we built AgentCore. AgentCore is a platform to build, connect, and optimize agents at scale with security enforced at the infrastructure layer that agents cannot bypass. AgentCore provides the foundational services needed for agents to operate in production: identity access control, runtime execution, tool connectivity, memory, observability, and many more. And it is open by design: any model, any framework, any protocol. Whether you're launching your first agent or your thousandth, you will have the system that scales with you, eliminates the infrastructure complexity while maintaining enterprise-grade security and reliability. And we are not stopping here. In the past few weeks we have launched significant new capabilities to AgentCore. AgentCore Policy and Evaluation is now generally available. And we have launched several new services in preview. Agent Registry helps you discover, govern, and reuse agents across the organization. AgentCore Harness helps you define an agent by specifying the model, instructions, and tools and run it immediately with zero orchestration code. And AgentCore Optimization helps you complete the loop of evaluate, observe, and improve, delivering recommendations to boost agent performance and quality and allows you with two methods of validation. Let's see how our customers are innovating with it. Customers across the industry such as Cox Automotive, Thomson Reuters, and PGA Tour are building with AgentCore. PGA Tour is transforming the content system and live play monitoring. And we have Cox Automotive who are deploying AI agents for fleet automation, optimization, vehicle shopping, and auction handling. And these aren't just isolated experiments. These are areas where we are continuously innovating and it represents a broader shift in the industry. And to understand why this moment is different, it helps to look at how agents themselves have evolved. Today agents accomplish tasks by dynamically discovering and composing tools, calling APIs, querying databases, pulling content, and invoking services all in real time. But here's what is changing. Not all of these tools and services are free of charge. As agents begin to consume paid endpoints, licensed content, and premium services as part of their execution loop, they just don't need the ability to act. They need the ability to pay. Tool providers, content platforms, and API services are well suited for pay-per-use pricing built for agentic consumption. We are talking about fractions of a cent per call billed in real time and no human in the loop. We see a future where billions of agents are autonomously making micro-transactions to access paid endpoints and content, all in real time. But here's the problem. Today when an agent hits a paywall, it stops. Not because it cannot reason through the task, but because it has no way to pay. The payment systems we rely on today are designed for humans: clicking buttons, confirming pop-ups, and entering card numbers. They were never designed for autonomous agents making thousands of sub-dollar transactions per hour. Let me put that into perspective. Every paid service your agent needs access to requires a separate account, an API key, and a billing relationship. If your agent touches 20 services, that is 20 bespoke integrations, each with its own registration, credential management, and invoicing. The complexity does not scale linearly; it compounds. And once agents are transacting autonomously, the governance challenges multiply. Spending limits need to be enforced per session, per user, per time window, and they need to be enforced deterministically, not left to the agent's judgment. Because when an agent spends money on your behalf, you need full observability: what it spent, why it spent, and whether it should have. Add it all up, and you're talking about months of engineering effort to bring this all together with deep payment expertise, and the stakes are high. A misconfigured payment flow doesn't just produce a bad answer; it moves real money. Credential exposure, unauthorized transactions, and uncontrolled spending are risks that require security to be built into the payment lifecycle from the start and not layered on. And what we keep hearing from developers is simple and direct: "Give me a solution that does all of this. Connects to wallets, orchestrates payments, enforces spending limits, and provides full observability—and don't make me build this from scratch or choose a protocol." In a world where every autonomous agent carries regulatory obligations, the infrastructure must be right from the get-go. And that's exactly the problem we have set out to solve. I'm excited to announce the launch of Amazon Bedrock AgentCore Payments, a new capability available in preview starting today. And we have built this in partnership with Coinbase, which provides the wallet and the payment facilitation, and Stripe, who provide the wallet support. So what does this unlock? Agents can now autonomously access and pay for APIs, MCP servers, web content, and other agents all within the execution loop. Developers don't need to adopt a separate payment stack. They continue building on the same AgentCore platform with the same identity, policy, gateway, and observability that they already rely on. This integration is what makes it powerful; payments isn't a bolt-on. So, how does this work? The payment flow is built on X-402, an open HTTP-native protocol for instant stablecoin micro-payments. When an agent hits a paid endpoint and it receives the HTTP 402 payment required response, the system authenticates the configured wallet, executes the payment, attaches proof, and delivers content all within the execution loop. With AgentCore Payments, developers can enable agentic payments in a few minutes with just a few lines of code. You can also set this up on the agent console. On the console, you will create a payment manager that helps you create a top-level resource that coordinates all the payment operations for your agent. You assign an IAM role or a JWT token for controlling access. Then you attach payment connectors such as Coinbase or Stripe preview. Credentials are stored in agent identity, the same system handling the agent authentication. AgentCore handles the auth and the token lifecycle automatically. A few clicks and a few minutes, and your agent is ready to transact. The payment manager orchestrates the payment flow, payment limits enforce session budgets, and every transaction is fully traceable on the AgentCore console. Now let me bring this into financial services specifically because this is where it gets powerful. Imagine a research agent tasked with analyzing stock performance. It needs access to real-time prices, earnings data, and analyst sentiment, and all of these are sitting behind paid endpoints. Without payment capability, the agents return stale or incomplete answers or developers need to build specific billing relationships. With AgentCore Payments, the agent now hits the paid endpoint and retrieves the cost of the assets and the instructions to pay. The agent then authenticates via the configured wallet, checks for the budget availability, makes the specific payment for each transaction, and attaches proof of the payment. The merchant endpoint validates the payment and delivers content, all of this happening within the agent execution loop. When the budget runs out, the payment is denied at the infrastructure layer. The agent doesn't decide whether to respect the limit; AgentCore enforces it. No runaway spend, no override. So what was once a maze of credentials, seat licenses, and access management is now fully automated in a budget-controlled workflow, purpose-built for agentic AI. And where are we headed? The builders we are working with span across the entire industry. Because anywhere you hit an agent paywall, whether it is licensed research, proprietary datasets, or metered APIs, the problem is the same. And as the payment platform evolves, so does the scope. Beyond micro-payments, the same infrastructure will support and power end-user commercial transactions. This includes tasks such as booking flights, reserving hotels, and completing purchases across different merchant platforms. The problem is universal and now so is the solution. The developer experience stays consistent across all of these phases. What changes isn't how you build; it's the breadth of what your agents can pay for. And we are not building it alone. You will hear from our launch partners Coinbase and Stripe, presenting today's agenda on how we are partnering with them to power the future of agentic commerce. And if the research agent resonated with you, make sure you visit the AWS booth at the expo area to dive deeper into these capabilities. Enterprises across media, financial services, and legal including Warner Brothers Discovery, National Australia Bank, LexisNexis, and Heurist AI are actively exploring how AgentCore Payments can power their agentic commerce strategies. AgentCore Payments gives these organizations the governed, scalable infrastructure they need to transact seamlessly in the moments that matter the most. Micro-payments is the first step. We are starting where we see the strongest pull: agent-to-agent commerce. But the vision extends well beyond that. We are looking toward deeper integration with payment ecosystem partners, support for additional protocols, buyer intent verification, and end-to-end observability across the full transaction lifecycle. And that's what we are building towards. AgentCore is used to deploy an agent every 10 seconds. That's not a statistic, it's a signal. A signal that the era of agentic AI isn't coming; it's here. Agents that can reason, plan, and act can now transact. And at AWS we are not building for where the industry is; we are building for where we are going from here. And we will be continuing to innovate on your behalf every step of the way. So let's build this future together. Thank you very much. Thank you! Financial Services Symposium 2026. Secretary Hegseth and Chairman Caine Testify Before the SAC FY 27 Budget Request

Department of War(332.0K)·124:33·5.2K views·2026-05-12
Pete HegsethSecretary, Department of War Dan CaineChairman, Department of War
TL;DRSecretary of War Pete Hegseth and Joint Chiefs Chairman Gen.
Dan Caine testified on the Department of War's $1.5 trillion FY27 budget request, defending the administration's military campaign in Iran and its transition to a "business model" for defense acquisitions....

TL;DR

Secretary of War Pete Hegseth and Joint Chiefs Chairman Gen. Dan Caine testified on the Department of War's $1.5 trillion FY27 budget request, defending the administration's military campaign in Iran and its transition to a "business model" for defense acquisitions. However, they faced intense bipartisan questioning over a $350 billion reliance on one-time reconciliation funding, rising domestic energy costs from the Strait of Hormuz blockade, and months-long delays in releasing congressional security assistance to Ukraine and Baltic allies.

The FY27 Budget Structure: Base vs. Reconciliation

  • The $1.5 Trillion Split: The Department of War is requesting $1.1 trillion in base appropriations and an additional $350 billion through a one-time budget reconciliation request.
  • Bipartisan Budgetary Concerns: Senate Appropriations Committee members strongly questioned why key, long-term military priorities are built into the volatile reconciliation request instead of the stable base budget.
      • Program Vulnerabilities: High-priority items dependent on the $350 billion reconciliation package include 53 of the requested 85 F-35 fighter jets, $53 billion for drone dominance, and $113 billion for munitions production and industrial base investments.
      • Lack of Industry Stability: Senators emphasized that base funding is critical for creating budget stability and sending consistent demand signals to the defense industrial base, whereas reconciliation is highly vulnerable to political gridlock.
  • Troop Quality of Life: Secretary Hegseth highlighted that the FY27 request includes a historic 7% troop pay increase and fully funds the elimination of all poor or failing barracks.

Military Operations in Iran and the Strait of Hormuz Blockade

  • Escalating Campaign Costs: The official operational cost estimate for Operation Epic Fury and Operation Freedom Now has risen to $29 billion.
      • Underreported Damage: Critics pointed out this estimate excludes the extensive cost of repairing damaged US facilities. Independent reports indicate Iran has struck at least 228 structures or pieces of equipment across 16 US military sites.
  • Strategic Success vs. Domestic Fallout: Gen. Caine outlined the military's objectives as sinking the conventional Iranian Navy, degrading ballistic missile/drone capabilities, and destroying Iran's defense industrial base to prevent them from obtaining a nuclear weapon.
      • The Strait of Hormuz Standoff: Despite the destruction of Iran's conventional navy, Iran's asymmetric use of fast boats and cheap Shahed drones has brought maritime shipping to a standstill, leaving 1,500 commercial tankers stranded.
      • US Energy Impact: US consumers are facing gas prices ranging from $4 to $7 per gallon due to the shipping disruptions. Hegseth defended the US naval blockade on Iranian ports, asserting that economic pressure on Tehran is overwhelming and that the US maintains the military means to reopen the strait.
  • Battle Damage Assessment Discrepancies: Senators challenged the administration's claim that 80% of Iran's missile capacity has been destroyed, citing intelligence estimates indicating that up to 70% of Iran's missile and drone capabilities remain intact. Both Hegseth and Caine declined to validate or comment on these figures in an open setting.

Allied Burden Sharing and Delayed Ukraine Aid

  • Frustration Over Delayed Ukraine Funding: Senators of both parties demanded to know why the department has failed to spend $400 million in European capacity-building funds appropriated by Congress in January to aid Ukraine.
      • Spend Plan Delays: Hegseth testified that the funds have been released from his office and that a final spending plan—formulated by European Command (EUCOM)—is expected to be delivered to Congress "this week."
  • Baltic Security Initiative (BSI) Halt: The department confirmed that $200 million designated for the Baltic states and Poland is currently frozen. The department is attempting to resolve an administrative drafting error in the appropriations bill through a reprogramming action to transfer the funds to the Defense Security Cooperation Agency (DSCA).
  • The Role of Global Alliances: Hegseth emphasized a focus on "burden sharing," stating that the US wants capable allies willing to deploy without political restrictions, rather than "carrying the burden for other countries." Senators Coons and Reed pushed back, warning that launching the war in Iran without consulting NATO partners has created a dangerous rift at a time when adversaries like Russia, China, North Korea, and Iran are increasingly aligned.

Defense Industrial Base and Shipbuilding Transitions

  • Pivot to a "Business Model": Secretary Hegseth testified that the Department of War has shifted its procurement strategy from a bureaucratic model to an outcome-driven business model.
      • Private Investment Surge: The department has stimulated over $50 billion in private investments across 39 states and 180 cities, resulting in 280 new or expanded manufacturing facilities, 18 million square feet of new manufacturing space, and 70,000 new jobs without utilizing taxpayer capital.
  • Domestic Shipbuilding Cutbacks: Senator Collins questioned why the department requested funding for only one DDG-51 Arleigh Burke-class destroyer (down from two in FY26) while allocating $1.8 billion in reconciliation funds for foreign-built surface combatants. Hegseth cited domestic shipyard capacity limits as the reason for the reduction.
  • Wisconsin Frigate Cancellation: Senator Baldwin pressed leadership over the abrupt cancellation of the Constellation-class frigate program in Wisconsin, which has disrupted the local workforce. She criticized the department's decision to place the construction of six replacement medium landing ships into the uncertain reconciliation budget.

***

Notable Quotes

"We have flipped Pentagon acquisition processes from a bureaucratic model to a business model. Decisively moving from an acquisitions environment paralyzed by bureaucratic red tape to an outcome-driven organization focused on delivering the most for taxpayers." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
"Our military objectives have been clear the whole time, around targeting Iran's ballistic missile systems, preventing them from threatening US forces in the region, destroying the Iranian Navy, degrading its capacity and capability, and ensuring that they can't rebuild by focusing on their defense industrial base." — Gen. Dan Caine
"The world of warfare is changing. Every major service can and should embrace smaller, lighter, faster, more distributed, lethal capabilities that will mostly be autonomous. This move towards a Golden Fleet, towards a new battleship, strikes me as moving in exactly the wrong direction, giving our adversary a bigger target rather than a more capable platform." — Senator Chris Coons
"America first does not have to mean America alone. We need all the friends we can get. They need to carry their own weight, they need to pay their bills, but the more the better." — Senator John Kennedy
Committee, come to order. Secretary Hegseth, General Caine, welcome. We look forward to discussing the President's budget, and we appreciate the opportunity to do that today. Obviously, we have very good attendance. Today, the need for significant defense investments is as urgent and obvious as it is overdue. Quantity has a quality of its own, and the sheer scale of our defense commitments sends a powerful signal. But what goes under the top line also matters. The way this budget request is structured matters. This is not a 1.5 trillion dollar defense appropriation request; it's a request for 1.1 trillion in base appropriations and, regretfully, another 350 billion in reconciliation. The good news is, the base request reflects real growth, unlike the net cut in the fiscal year 26 request, and very much unlike the Biden level CR in 25. But some of the most pressing items on the fiscal 27 to-do list are directly downstream of missed opportunities in fiscal 26. For example, like the failure to fully fund 28 billion in multiyear contracts for critical munitions. The subcommittee did what we could to increase munitions purchases above the fiscal 26 request levels, but we couldn't do it all without a sufficient top line. This year, the fiscal 27 request sets a higher top line—high enough, one would think, to build the department's highest priorities into the base budget request, instead of shunning them off into a one-time reconciliation request. Which brings me to the bad news. The 27 request fails to make room in the base budget for some of the military's top priorities. The distinction between base and reconciliation really matters. Base funding is what creates budget stability for the services and sends consistent demand signals to industry. And base funding is what gets extended by short-term continuing resolutions when work on a full-year appropriations is unfinished. As I said last year, reconciliation should be a supplement to, not a substitute for. Political realities will not always allow for party line budget reconciliation. And if the department's top priorities aren't built into annual appropriations, we're actually taking a big risk. The department is right to make Golden Dome munitions and the F-35 program and drone dominance top priorities. But these key lines of effort only work if we put them on solid fiscal footing. So, why is the department requesting funding for multiyear munitions contracts, which by definition require steady, year-on-year funding, via a one-time reconciliation? And why risk some of the president's top priorities like Golden Dome by not firmly building them into the department's baseline? National missile defense will require sustained funding over many years, not a one-year expenditure. Other key platforms, like the second destroyer and the E-7 battle management aircraft, aren't requested in base or reconciliation. Our adversaries are working together to undermine America and the West. Strained relations with our partners, who are making generational commitments to collective defense and driving investment into American-made weapons and systems, only serves our adversaries' interest and limits our capacity and deterrent power globally. From the Baltic states to Taiwan and the Philippines, I expect the European capacity building investments intended specifically for Ukraine reach their destination without further delay. When our partners are capable, deterrent is stronger, and the risk to our own service members is lower. This is true in the Middle East today, as it was in Europe and the Indo-Pacific.
